	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Salvador Dalí</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Salvador_Dal%C3%AD_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Salvador Dalí (disambiguation)">Salvador Dalí (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Spanish artist</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article uses <a href="/wiki/Spanish_naming_customs" title="Spanish naming customs">Spanish naming customs</a>: the first or paternal <a href="/wiki/Surname" title="Surname">family name</a> is <i>Dalí</i>&#32;and the second or maternal family name is <i>Domènech</i>.</div>

Dalí</div></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Salvador_Dal%C3%AD_1939.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Salvador Dalí 1939.jpg" class="category">Spanish</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Education</th><td><a href="/wiki/Real_Academia_de_Bellas_Artes_de_San_Fernando" title="Real Academia de Bellas Artes de San Fernando">San Fernando School of Fine Arts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">Madrid</a>, Spain</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Known&#160;for</th><td><a href="/wiki/Painting" title="Painting">Painting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drawing" title="Drawing">drawing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Photography" title="Photography">photography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sculpture" title="Sculpture">sculpture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">writing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Film" title="Film">film</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jewelry" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewelry">jewelry</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><div <li><i><a href="/wiki/Dream_Caused_by_the_Flight_of_a_Bee_Around_a_Pomegranate_a_Second_Before_Awakening" title="Dream Caused by the Flight of a Bee Around a Pomegranate a Second Before Awakening">Dream Caused by the Flight of a Bee Around a Pomegranate a Second Before Awakening</a></i> (1944)</li>

</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Movement</th><td class="category"><a href="/wiki/Cubism" title="Cubism">Cubism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dada" title="Dada">Dada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surrealism" title="Surrealism">Surrealism</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><span class="nowrap">Spouse(s)</span></th><td><div style="display:inline-block;line-height:normal;margin:2px 0px;"><a href="/wiki/Gala_Dal%C3%AD" title="Gala Dalí">Gala Dalí</a> (Elena Ivanovna Diakonova)<br />&#40;<abbr title="married">m.</abbr>&#160;1934&#59;&#32;<abbr title="died">d.</abbr>&#160;1982&#41;</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Salvador Domingo Felipe Jacinto Dalí i Domènech, Marquis of Dalí de Púbol</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/i/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;happy&#39;">i</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/iː/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">iː</span></span>/</a></span></span>,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> <small>Catalan:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Catalan" title="Help:IPA/Catalan">[səlβəˈðo ðəˈli]</a></span>, <small>Spanish:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Spanish" title="Help:IPA/Spanish">[salβaˈðoɾ ðaˈli]</a></span>; 11 May 1904&#160;&#8211;&#32;23 January 1989) was a Spanish <a href="/wiki/Surrealism" title="Surrealism">Surrealist</a> painter born in <a href="/wiki/Figueres" title="Figueres">Figueres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a>, Spain.
</p><p>Dalí was a skilled <a href="/wiki/Technical_drawing" title="Technical drawing">draftsman</a>, best known for the striking and bizarre images in his work. His <a href="/wiki/Painterliness" title="Painterliness">painterly</a> skills are often attributed to the influence of <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_art" title="Renaissance art">Renaissance</a> masters.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dali_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dali-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> His best-known work, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Persistence_of_Memory" title="The Persistence of Memory">The Persistence of Memory</a></i>, was completed in August, 1931, and is one of the most recognisable Surrealist paintings. Dalí's expansive artistic repertoire included film, sculpture, and photography, at times in collaboration with a range of artists in a variety of media.
</p><p>Dalí was highly imaginative, and also enjoyed indulging in unusual and grandiose behavior. To the dismay of those who held his work in high regard, and to the irritation of his critics, his eccentric manner and attention-grabbing public actions sometimes drew more attention than his artwork.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meisler_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meisler-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Familia_Dal%C3%AD_(h_1910).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Familia_Dal%C3%AD_%28h_1910%29.jpg/220px-Familia_Dal%C3%AD_%28h_1910%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Familia_Dal%C3%AD_%28h_1910%29.jpg/330px-Familia_Dal%C3%AD_%28h_1910%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Familia_Dal%C3%AD_%28h_1910%29.jpg/440px-Familia_Dal%C3%AD_%28h_1910%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="688" data-file-height="618" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Familia_Dal%C3%AD_(h_1910).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Dalí family in 1910: from the upper left, aunt Maria Teresa, mother, father, Salvador Dalí, aunt Caterina (later became second wife of father), sister Anna Maria and grandmother Anna</div></div></div>
<p>Salvador Dalí was born on 11 May 1904, at 8:45&#160;am GMT,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (May 2019)">failed verification</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> on the first floor of Carrer Monturiol, 20 in the town of Figueres, in the <a href="/wiki/Empord%C3%A0" title="Empordà">Empordà</a> <a href="/wiki/Comarques_of_Catalonia" title="Comarques of Catalonia">region</a>, close to the French border in <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a>, Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Dalí's older brother, who had also been <a href="/wiki/Necronym" title="Necronym">named Salvador</a> (born 12 October 1901), had died of <a href="/wiki/Gastroenteritis" title="Gastroenteritis">gastroenteritis</a> nine months earlier, on 1 August 1903. His father, Salvador Rafael Aniceto Dalí Cusí (1872–1950)<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> was a middle-class lawyer and notary,<sup id="cite_ref-Llongueras_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Llongueras-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> an anti-clerical atheist and Catalan federalist, whose strict disciplinary approach was tempered by his wife, Felipa Domènech Ferrés (1874–1921),<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> who encouraged her son's artistic endeavors.<sup id="cite_ref-Rojas_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rojas-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In the summer of 1912, the family moved to the top floor of Carrer Monturiol 24 (presently 10).<sup id="cite_ref-Gibson,_Ian_1997_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gibson,_Ian_1997-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Dalí later attributed his "love of everything that is gilded and excessive, my passion for luxury and my love of oriental clothes"<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> to an "Arab lineage", claiming that his ancestors were descendants of the <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Meisler_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meisler-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-isbn0-571-19380-3_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-571-19380-3-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a child Dalí was taken to his brother's grave and told by his parents that he was his brother's <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-sina_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sina-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> a concept which he came to believe.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Of his brother, Dalí said, "[we] resembled each other like two drops of water, but we had different reflections."<sup id="cite_ref-Dalí,_Secret_Life,_p.2_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dalí,_Secret_Life,_p.2-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> He "was probably a first version of myself but conceived too much in the absolute."<sup id="cite_ref-Dalí,_Secret_Life,_p.2_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dalí,_Secret_Life,_p.2-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Images of his long-dead brother would reappear embedded in his later works, including <i>Portrait of My Dead Brother</i> (1963).
</p><p>Dalí also had a sister, Anna Maria, who was three years younger.<sup id="cite_ref-Llongueras_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Llongueras-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In 1949, she published a book about her brother, <i>Dalí as Seen by His Sister</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>His childhood friends included future <a href="/wiki/FC_Barcelona" title="FC Barcelona">FC Barcelona</a> footballers <a href="/wiki/Emilio_Sagi_Li%C3%B1%C3%A1n" title="Emilio Sagi Liñán">Sagibarba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Josep_Samitier" title="Josep Samitier">Josep Samitier</a>. During holidays at the Catalan resort of <a href="/wiki/Cadaqu%C3%A9s" title="Cadaqués">Cadaqués</a>, the trio played <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a> together.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Dalí attended <a href="/wiki/Art_school" title="Art school">drawing school</a>. In 1916, he also discovered modern painting on a summer vacation trip to Cadaqués with the family of <a href="/wiki/Ramon_Pichot" title="Ramon Pichot">Ramon Pichot</a>, a local artist who made regular trips to Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-Llongueras_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Llongueras-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The next year, Dalí's father organized an exhibition of his charcoal drawings in their family home. He had his first public exhibition at the Municipal Theatre in Figueres in 1918,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> a site he would return to decades later.
</p><p>On 6 February 1921, Dalí's mother died of <a href="/wiki/Uterine_cancer" title="Uterine cancer">uterine cancer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Dalí was 16 years old; he later said his mother's death "was the greatest blow I had experienced in my life. I worshipped her... I could not resign myself to the loss of a being on whom I counted to make invisible the unavoidable blemishes of my soul."<sup id="cite_ref-Meisler_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meisler-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> After her death, Dalí's father married his deceased wife's sister. Dalí did not resent this marriage, because he had great love and respect for his aunt.<sup id="cite_ref-Llongueras_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Llongueras-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Madrid.2C_Barcelona_and_Paris"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Madrid,_Barcelona_and_Paris">Madrid, Barcelona and Paris</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Madrid, Barcelona and Paris">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Man_Ray_Salvador_Dali.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Man_Ray_Salvador_Dali.jpg/220px-Man_Ray_Salvador_Dali.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Man_Ray_Salvador_Dali.jpg/330px-Man_Ray_Salvador_Dali.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Man_Ray_Salvador_Dali.jpg/440px-Man_Ray_Salvador_Dali.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1505" data-file-height="983" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Man_Ray_Salvador_Dali.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dalí (left) and fellow <a href="/wiki/Surrealism" title="Surrealism">surrealist</a> artist <a href="/wiki/Man_Ray" title="Man Ray">Man Ray</a> in Paris on 16 June 1934</div></div></div>
<p>In 1922, Dalí moved into the <a href="/wiki/Residencia_de_Estudiantes" title="Residencia de Estudiantes">Residencia de Estudiantes</a> (Students' Residence) in <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">Madrid</a><sup id="cite_ref-Llongueras_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Llongueras-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and studied at the <a href="/wiki/Real_Academia_de_Bellas_Artes_de_San_Fernando" title="Real Academia de Bellas Artes de San Fernando">Real Academia de Bellas Artes de San Fernando</a>. A lean 1.72 metres (5&#160;ft <span class="frac nowrap">7<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>3</sup>&#8260;<sub>4</sub></span>&#160;in) tall,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Dalí already drew attention as an eccentric and <a href="/wiki/Dandy" title="Dandy">dandy</a>. He had long hair and <a href="/wiki/Sideburn" class="mw-redirect" title="Sideburn">sideburns</a>, coat, stockings, and <a href="/wiki/Knee-breeches" class="mw-redirect" title="Knee-breeches">knee-breeches</a> in the style of English <a href="/wiki/Artistic_dress_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Artistic dress movement">aesthetes</a> of the late 19th century.
</p><p>At the Residencia, he became close friends with (among others) <a href="/wiki/Pep%C3%ADn_Bello" class="mw-redirect" title="Pepín Bello">Pepín Bello</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luis_Bu%C3%B1uel" title="Luis Buñuel">Luis Buñuel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Federico_Garc%C3%ADa_Lorca" title="Federico García Lorca">Federico García Lorca</a>. The friendship with Lorca had a strong element of mutual passion,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> but Dalí rejected the poet's sexual advances.<sup id="cite_ref-conversations_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-conversations-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Salvador_Dal%C3%AD,_Federico_Garc%C3%ADa_Lorca,_Barcelona,_1925.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Salvador_Dal%C3%AD%2C_Federico_Garc%C3%ADa_Lorca%2C_Barcelona%2C_1925.jpg/220px-Salvador_Dal%C3%AD%2C_Federico_Garc%C3%ADa_Lorca%2C_Barcelona%2C_1925.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="348" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Salvador_Dal%C3%AD%2C_Federico_Garc%C3%ADa_Lorca%2C_Barcelona%2C_1925.jpg/330px-Salvador_Dal%C3%AD%2C_Federico_Garc%C3%ADa_Lorca%2C_Barcelona%2C_1925.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Salvador_Dal%C3%AD%2C_Federico_Garc%C3%ADa_Lorca%2C_Barcelona%2C_1925.jpg/440px-Salvador_Dal%C3%AD%2C_Federico_Garc%C3%ADa_Lorca%2C_Barcelona%2C_1925.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2722" data-file-height="4308" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Salvador_Dal%C3%AD,_Federico_Garc%C3%ADa_Lorca,_Barcelona,_1925.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dalí with <a href="/wiki/Federico_Garc%C3%ADa_Lorca" title="Federico García Lorca">Federico García Lorca</a>, Turó Park de la Guineueta, Barcelona, 1925</div></div></div>
<p>It was his paintings in which he experimented with <a href="/wiki/Cubism" title="Cubism">Cubism</a>, however, that earned him the most attention from his fellow students. Since there were no Cubist artists in Madrid at the time, his knowledge of Cubist art had come from magazine articles and a catalog given to him by Pichot.
</p><p>Dalí, still unknown to the public, illustrated a book for the first time in 1924. It was a publication of the <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">Catalan</a> poem <i>Les bruixes de <a href="/wiki/Llers" title="Llers">Llers</a></i> ("The Witches of Llers") by his friend and schoolmate, poet <a href="/wiki/Carles_Fages_de_Climent" title="Carles Fages de Climent">Carles Fages de Climent</a>. Dalí also experimented with <a href="/wiki/Dada" title="Dada">Dada</a>, which influenced his work throughout his life.<sup id="cite_ref-Dali_Dada_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dali_Dada-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dalí held his first solo exhibition at <a href="/wiki/Galeries_Dalmau" title="Galeries Dalmau">Galeries Dalmau</a> in Barcelona, from 14 to 27 November 1925.<sup id="cite_ref-Fèlix_Fanés_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fèlix_Fanés-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> At the time Dalí was not yet immersed in the <a href="/wiki/Surrealism" title="Surrealism">Surrealist</a> style for which he would later become famous. The exhibition was well received by the public and critics. The following year he exhibited again at Galeries Dalmau, from 31 December 1926 to 14 January 1927, with the support of the art critic <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sebasti%C3%A0_Gasch&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sebastià Gasch (page does not exist)">Sebastià Gasch</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sebasti%C3%A0_Gasch" class="extiw" title="es:Sebastià Gasch">es</a>&#93;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pàmies_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pàmies-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dalí left the Academy in 1926, shortly before his final exams.<sup id="cite_ref-Meisler_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meisler-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> His mastery of painting skills at that time was evidenced by his realistic <i><a href="/wiki/The_Basket_of_Bread" title="The Basket of Bread">The Basket of Bread</a></i>, painted in 1926.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> That same year, he made his first visit to Paris, where he met <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">Pablo Picasso</a>, whom the young Dalí revered.<sup id="cite_ref-Meisler_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meisler-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Picasso had already heard favorable reports about Dalí from <a href="/wiki/Joan_Mir%C3%B3" title="Joan Miró">Joan Miró</a>, a fellow Catalan who introduced him to many Surrealist friends.<sup id="cite_ref-Meisler_6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meisler-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> As he developed his own style over the next few years, Dalí made a number of works strongly influenced by Picasso and Miró.
</p><p>Some trends in Dalí's work that would continue throughout his life were already evident in the 1920s. Dalí was influenced by many styles of art, ranging from the most academically classic, to the most cutting-edge <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">avant-garde</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> His classical influences included <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronzino" title="Bronzino">Bronzino</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Zurbar%C3%A1n" title="Francisco de Zurbarán">Francisco de Zurbarán</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Vermeer" title="Johannes Vermeer">Vermeer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez" title="Diego Velázquez">Velázquez</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> He used both classical and modernist techniques, sometimes in separate works, and sometimes combined. Exhibitions of his works in <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">Barcelona</a> attracted much attention and a mixture of praise and puzzled debate from critics.
</p><p>Dalí grew a flamboyant <a href="/wiki/Moustache" title="Moustache">moustache</a>, influenced by 17th-century Spanish master painter <a href="/wiki/Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez" title="Diego Velázquez">Diego Velázquez</a>. This moustache became a well-known trademark of his appearance for the rest of his life.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1929_to_World_War_II">1929 to World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1929 to World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Salvador_Dal%C3%AD,_1934_(photo_by_Carl_Van_Vechten).jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Salvador_Dal%C3%AD%2C_1934_%28photo_by_Carl_Van_Vechten%29.jpeg/220px-Salvador_Dal%C3%AD%2C_1934_%28photo_by_Carl_Van_Vechten%29.jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/34/Salvador_Dal%C3%AD%2C_1934_%28photo_by_Carl_Van_Vechten%29.jpeg 1.5x" data-file-width="322" data-file-height="403" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Salvador_Dal%C3%AD,_1934_(photo_by_Carl_Van_Vechten).jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Salvador Dalí</div></div></div>
<p>In 1929, Dalí collaborated with surrealist film director <a href="/wiki/Luis_Bu%C3%B1uel" title="Luis Buñuel">Luis Buñuel</a> on the short film <i lang="fr" title="French language text"><a href="/wiki/Un_Chien_Andalou" title="Un Chien Andalou">Un Chien Andalou</a></i> (<i>An Andalusian Dog</i>). His main contribution was to help Buñuel write the script for the film. Dalí later claimed to have also played a significant role in the filming of the project, but this is not substantiated by contemporary accounts.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Also, in August 1929, Dalí met his lifelong and primary <a href="/wiki/Muse" class="mw-redirect" title="Muse">muse</a> and future wife <a href="/wiki/Gala_Dal%C3%AD" title="Gala Dalí">Gala</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-unbound_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unbound-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> born Elena Ivanovna Diakonova. She was a Russian immigrant ten years his senior, who at that time was married to surrealist poet <a href="/wiki/Paul_%C3%89luard" title="Paul Éluard">Paul Éluard</a>. In the same year, Dalí had important professional exhibitions and officially joined the Surrealist group in the <a href="/wiki/Montparnasse" title="Montparnasse">Montparnasse</a> quarter of Paris. His work had already been heavily influenced by surrealism for two years. The Surrealists hailed what Dalí called his <a href="/wiki/Paranoiac-critical_method" title="Paranoiac-critical method">paranoiac-critical method</a> of accessing the <a href="/wiki/Subconscious" title="Subconscious">subconscious</a> for greater artistic creativity.<sup id="cite_ref-Llongueras_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Llongueras-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rojas_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rojas-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, Dalí's relationship with his father was close to rupture. Don Salvador Dalí y Cusi strongly disapproved of his son's romance with Gala, and saw his connection to the Surrealists as a bad influence on his morals. The final straw was when Don Salvador read in a Barcelona newspaper that his son had recently exhibited in Paris a drawing of the <i>Sacred Heart of Jesus Christ</i>, with a provocative inscription: "Sometimes, I spit for fun on my mother's portrait".<sup id="cite_ref-Meisler_6-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meisler-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-isbn0-571-19380-3_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-571-19380-3-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Outraged, Don Salvador demanded that his son recant publicly. Dalí refused, perhaps out of fear of expulsion from the Surrealist group, and was violently thrown out of his paternal home on 28 December 1929. His father told him that he would be <a href="/wiki/Inheritance" title="Inheritance">disinherited</a>, and that he should never set foot in Cadaqués again. The following summer, Dalí and Gala rented a small fisherman's cabin in a nearby bay at <a href="/wiki/Port_Lligat" class="mw-redirect" title="Port Lligat">Port Lligat</a>. He bought the place, and over the years enlarged it by buying the neighbouring fishermen cabins, gradually building his much beloved <a href="/wiki/Villa" title="Villa">villa</a> by the sea. Dalí's father would eventually relent and come to accept his son's companion.<sup id="cite_ref-GalaGSDF_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GalaGSDF-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1931, Dalí painted one of his most famous works, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Persistence_of_Memory" title="The Persistence of Memory">The Persistence of Memory</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> which introduced a surrealistic image of soft, melting <a href="/wiki/Pocket_watch" title="Pocket watch">pocket watches</a>. The general interpretation of the work is that the soft watches are a rejection of the assumption that time is rigid or <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">deterministic</a>. This idea is supported by other images in the work, such as the wide expanding landscape, and other limp watches shown being devoured by ants.<sup id="cite_ref-Conquete_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conquete-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dalí and Gala, having lived together since 1929, were civilly married on 30 January 1934 in Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> They later remarried in a Church ceremony on 8 August 1958 at <a href="/wiki/Sant_Mart%C3%AD_Vell" title="Sant Martí Vell">Sant Martí Vell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> In addition to inspiring many artworks throughout her life, Gala would act as Dalí's <a href="/wiki/Business_manager" title="Business manager">business manager</a>, supporting their extravagant lifestyle while adeptly steering clear of insolvency. Gala seemed to tolerate Dalí's dalliances with younger muses, secure in her own position as his primary relationship. Dalí continued to paint her as they both aged, producing sympathetic and adoring images of her. The "tense, complex and ambiguous relationship" lasting over 50 years would later become the subject of an opera, <i>Jo, Dalí</i> (<i>I, Dalí</i>) by <a href="/wiki/Catalan_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalan people">Catalan</a> composer Xavier Benguerel.<sup id="cite_ref-Opera_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Opera-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dalí was introduced to the United States by art dealer <a href="/wiki/Julien_Levy" title="Julien Levy">Julien Levy</a> in 1934. The exhibition in New York of Dalí's works, including <i>Persistence of Memory</i>, created an immediate sensation. <a href="/wiki/Social_Register" title="Social Register">Social Register</a> listees feted him at a specially organized "Dalí Ball". He showed up wearing a glass case on his chest, which contained a brassiere.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In that year, Dalí and Gala also attended a masquerade party in New York, hosted for them by heiress <a href="/wiki/Caresse_Crosby" title="Caresse Crosby">Caresse Crosby</a>, the inventor of the brassiere. For their costumes, they dressed as the <a href="/wiki/Lindbergh_baby" class="mw-redirect" title="Lindbergh baby">Lindbergh baby</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Hauptmann" class="mw-redirect" title="Bruno Hauptmann">kidnapper</a>. The resulting uproar in the press was so great that Dalí apologized. When he returned to Paris, the Surrealists confronted him about his apology for a surrealist act.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While the majority of the Surrealist artists had become increasingly associated with <a href="/wiki/Leftist" class="mw-redirect" title="Leftist">leftist</a> politics, Dalí maintained an ambiguous position on the subject of the proper relationship between politics and art. Leading surrealist <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Breton" title="André Breton">André Breton</a> accused Dalí of defending the "new" and "irrational" in "the Hitler phenomenon", but Dalí quickly rejected this claim, saying, "I am Hitlerian neither in fact nor intention".<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Dalí insisted that surrealism could exist in an apolitical context and refused to explicitly denounce <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">fascism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Among other factors, this had landed him in trouble with his colleagues. Later in 1934, Dalí was subjected to a "trial", in which he narrowly avoided being expelled from the Surrealist group.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> To this, Dalí retorted, "The difference between the surrealists and me is, I myself am surrealism" (<i>la différence entre les surréalistes et moi, c'est que moi je suis surréaliste</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1936, Dalí took part in the <a href="/wiki/London_International_Surrealist_Exhibition" title="London International Surrealist Exhibition">London International Surrealist Exhibition</a>. His lecture, titled <i lang="fr" title="French language text">Fantômes paranoiaques authentiques</i>, was delivered while wearing a deep-sea <a href="/wiki/Diving_suit" title="Diving suit">diving suit</a> and helmet.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> He had arrived carrying a <a href="/wiki/Billiard_cue" class="mw-redirect" title="Billiard cue">billiard cue</a> and leading a pair of <a href="/wiki/Russian_wolfhound" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian wolfhound">Russian wolfhounds</a>, and had to have the helmet unscrewed as he gasped for breath. He commented that "I just wanted to show that I was 'plunging deeply' into the human mind."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In 1936, Dalí, aged 32, was featured on the cover of <i><a href="/wiki/Time_magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="Time magazine">Time</a></i> magazine.<sup id="cite_ref-Meisler_6-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meisler-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Also in 1936, at the premiere screening of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Cornell" title="Joseph Cornell">Joseph Cornell</a>'s film <i><a href="/wiki/Rose_Hobart_(film)" title="Rose Hobart (film)">Rose Hobart</a></i> at Julien Levy's gallery in New York City, Dalí became famous for another incident. Levy's program of short surrealist films was timed to take place at the same time as the first surrealism exhibition at the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Modern_Art" title="Museum of Modern Art">Museum of Modern Art</a>, featuring Dalí's work. Dalí was in the audience at the screening, but halfway through the film, he knocked over the projector in a rage. "My idea for a film is exactly that, and I was going to propose it to someone who would pay to have it made", he said. "I never wrote it down or told anyone, but it is as if he had stolen it". Other versions of Dalí's accusation tend to the more poetic: "He stole it from my subconscious!" or even "He stole my dreams!"<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In this period, Dalí's main patron in London was the wealthy <a href="/wiki/Edward_James" title="Edward James">Edward James</a>. He had helped Dalí emerge into the art world by purchasing many works and by supporting him financially for two years. They also collaborated on two of the most enduring icons of the Surrealist movement: the <i><a href="/wiki/Lobster_Telephone" title="Lobster Telephone">Lobster Telephone</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Mae_West_Lips_Sofa" title="Mae West Lips Sofa">Mae West Lips Sofa</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, Spain was going through <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">a civil war</a> (1936–1939), with many artists taking a side or going into exile.
</p><p>In 1938, Dalí met <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a> thanks to <a href="/wiki/Stefan_Zweig" title="Stefan Zweig">Stefan Zweig</a>. Dalí started to sketch Freud's portrait, while the 82-year-old celebrity confided to others that "This boy looks like a fanatic." Dalí was delighted upon hearing later about this comment from his hero.<sup id="cite_ref-Meisler_6-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meisler-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Later, in September 1938, Salvador Dalí was invited by Gabrielle <a href="/wiki/Coco_Chanel" title="Coco Chanel">Coco Chanel</a> to her house "<a href="/wiki/La_Pausa" title="La Pausa">La Pausa</a>" in <a href="/wiki/Roquebrune-Cap-Martin" title="Roquebrune-Cap-Martin">Roquebrune</a> on the <a href="/wiki/French_Riviera" title="French Riviera">French Riviera</a>. There he painted numerous paintings he later exhibited at Julien Levy Gallery in New York.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> At the end of the 20th century, "La Pausa" was partially replicated at the <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Museum_of_Art" title="Dallas Museum of Art">Dallas Museum of Art</a> to welcome the Reeves collection and part of Chanel's original furniture from the house.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Also in 1938, Dalí unveiled <i><a href="/wiki/Rainy_Taxi" title="Rainy Taxi">Rainy Taxi</a></i>, a three-dimensional artwork, consisting of an actual automobile with two <a href="/wiki/Mannequin" title="Mannequin">mannequin</a> occupants. The piece was first displayed at the Galerie Beaux-Arts in Paris at the <a href="/wiki/Exposition_Internationale_du_Surr%C3%A9alisme" title="Exposition Internationale du Surréalisme">Exposition Internationale du Surréalisme</a>, organised by <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Breton" title="André Breton">André Breton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_%C3%89luard" title="Paul Éluard">Paul Éluard</a>. The Exposition was designed by artist <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Duchamp" title="Marcel Duchamp">Marcel Duchamp</a>, who also served as host.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the <a href="/wiki/1939_New_York_World%27s_Fair" title="1939 New York World&#39;s Fair">1939 New York World's Fair</a>, Dalí debuted his <i>Dream of Venus</i> surrealist pavilion, located in the Amusements Area of the exposition. It featured bizarre sculptures, statues, and live nude models in "costumes" made of fresh seafood, an event photographed by <a href="/wiki/Horst_P._Horst" title="Horst P. Horst">Horst P. Horst</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Platt_Lynes" title="George Platt Lynes">George Platt Lynes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Murray_Korman" title="Murray Korman">Murray Korman</a>. Like most attractions in the Amusements Area, an admission fee was charged.<sup id="cite_ref-DrmVns_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DrmVns-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1939, André Breton coined the derogatory nickname "Avida Dollars", an <a href="/wiki/Anagram" title="Anagram">anagram</a> for "Salvador Dalí", a phonetic rendering of the French phrase <i>avide à dollars</i>, meaning "eager for dollars".<sup id="cite_ref-artcyclopedia_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-artcyclopedia-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> This was a derisive reference to the increasing commercialization of Dalí's work, and the perception that Dalí sought self-aggrandizement through fame and fortune. The Surrealists, many of whom were closely connected to the <a href="/wiki/French_Communist_Party" title="French Communist Party">French Communist Party</a> at the time, expelled him from their movement.<sup id="cite_ref-Meisler_6-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meisler-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Some surrealists henceforth spoke of Dalí in the past tense, as if he were dead.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The Surrealist movement and various members thereof (such as <a href="/wiki/Ted_Joans" title="Ted Joans">Ted Joans</a>) would continue to issue extremely harsh <a href="/wiki/Polemics" class="mw-redirect" title="Polemics">polemics</a> against Dalí until the time of his death, and beyond.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1940, as <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> tore through Europe, Dalí and Gala retreated to the United States, where they lived for eight years splitting their time between New York and Monterey, California.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> They were able to escape because on June 20, 1940, they were issued visas by <a href="/wiki/Aristides_de_Sousa_Mendes" title="Aristides de Sousa Mendes">Aristides de Sousa Mendes</a>, Portuguese consul in Bordeaux, France. Salvador and Gala Dalí crossed into Portugal and subsequently sailed on the <i>Excambion</i> from Lisbon to New York in August 1940. Dalí's arrival in New York was one of the catalysts in the development of that city as a world art center in the <a href="/wiki/Post-war" title="Post-war">post-war</a> years.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>  After the move, Dalí returned to the practice of <a href="/wiki/Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholicism">Catholicism</a>. "During this period, Dalí never stopped writing", wrote Robert and Nicolas Descharnes.<sup id="cite_ref-Descharnes_(1993)_p._35._65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Descharnes_(1993)_p._35.-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dalí worked prolifically in a variety of media during this period, designing jewelry, clothes, furniture, stage sets for plays and ballet, and retail store display windows. In 1939, while working on a window display for <a href="/wiki/Bonwit_Teller" title="Bonwit Teller">Bonwit Teller</a>, he became so enraged by unauthorized changes to his work that he shoved a decorative bathtub through a plate glass window.<sup id="cite_ref-Meisler_6-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meisler-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dali spent the winter of 1940–41 at Hampton Manor, the residence of bra designer and patron of the arts <a href="/wiki/Caresse_Crosby" title="Caresse Crosby">Caresse Crosby</a>, near Bowling Green in <a href="/wiki/Caroline_County,_Virginia" title="Caroline County, Virginia">Caroline County, Virginia</a>. During his time there, he spent his time on various projects. He was described as a "showman" by residents in the local newspaper.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1941, Dalí drafted a film scenario for <a href="/wiki/Jean_Gabin" title="Jean Gabin">Jean Gabin</a> called <i>Moontide</i>. In 1942, he published his autobiography, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_Life_of_Salvador_Dal%C3%AD" title="The Secret Life of Salvador Dalí">The Secret Life of Salvador Dalí</a></i>. He wrote catalogs for his exhibitions, such as that at the Knoedler Gallery in New York in 1943, in which he attacked some often-used surrealist techniques by proclaiming, "Surrealism will at least have served to give experimental proof that total sterility and attempts at automatizations have gone too far and have led to a totalitarian system. ... Today's laziness and the total lack of technique have reached their paroxysm in the psychological signification of the current use of the college" (<a href="/wiki/Collage" title="Collage">collage</a>). He also wrote a novel, published in 1944, about a fashion salon for automobiles. This resulted in a drawing by Edwin Cox in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Miami_Herald" class="mw-redirect" title="The Miami Herald">The Miami Herald</a></i>, depicting Dalí dressing an automobile in an evening gown.<sup id="cite_ref-Descharnes_(1993)_p._35._65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Descharnes_(1993)_p._35.-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i>The Secret Life</i>, Dalí suggested that he had split with <a href="/wiki/Luis_Bu%C3%B1uel" title="Luis Buñuel">Luis Buñuel</a> because the latter was a <a href="/wiki/Communist" class="mw-redirect" title="Communist">Communist</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Atheist" class="mw-redirect" title="Atheist">atheist</a>. Buñuel was fired (or resigned) from his position at the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Modern_Art" title="Museum of Modern Art">Museum of Modern Art</a> (MOMA), supposedly after <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_Spellman" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardinal Spellman">Cardinal Spellman</a> of New York went to see <a href="/wiki/Iris_Barry" title="Iris Barry">Iris Barry</a>, head of the film department at MOMA. Buñuel then went back to Hollywood where he worked in the dubbing department of <a href="/wiki/Warner_Brothers" class="mw-redirect" title="Warner Brothers">Warner Brothers</a> from 1942 to 1946. In his 1982 autobiography <i>Mon Dernier soupir</i> (<i>My Last Sigh</i>, 1983), Buñuel wrote that, over the years, he had rejected Dalí's attempts at reconciliation.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An Italian <a href="/wiki/Friar" title="Friar">friar</a>, Gabriele Maria Berardi, claimed to have performed an <a href="/wiki/Exorcism" title="Exorcism">exorcism</a> on Dalí while he was in France in 1947.<sup id="cite_ref-exorcist-gift_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-exorcist-gift-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In 2005, a sculpture of Christ on the Cross was discovered in the friar's estate. It had been claimed that Dalí gave this work to his exorcist out of gratitude,<sup id="cite_ref-exorcist-gift_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-exorcist-gift-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> and two Spanish art experts confirmed that there were adequate stylistic reasons to believe the sculpture was made by Dalí.<sup id="cite_ref-exorcist-gift_68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-exorcist-gift-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_years_in_Spain">Later years in Spain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Later years in Spain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dali_Allan_Warren.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Dali_Allan_Warren.jpg/220px-Dali_Allan_Warren.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Dali_Allan_Warren.jpg/330px-Dali_Allan_Warren.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Dali_Allan_Warren.jpg/440px-Dali_Allan_Warren.jpg 2x" data-file-width="465" data-file-height="540" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dali_Allan_Warren.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Dalí by <a href="/wiki/Allan_Warren" title="Allan Warren">Allan Warren</a>, 1972</div></div></div>
<p>In 1948 Dalí and Gala moved back into their house in Port Lligat, on the coast near Cadaqués. For the next three decades, he would spend most of his time there painting, taking time off and spending winters with his wife in Paris and New York.<sup id="cite_ref-Meisler_6-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meisler-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GalaGSDF_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GalaGSDF-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> His decision to live in <a href="/wiki/Spain_under_Franco" class="mw-redirect" title="Spain under Franco">Spain under Franco</a> prompted disagreement from other Spanish artists and intellectuals who remained in exile.
</p><p>In 1959, <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Breton" title="André Breton">André Breton</a> organized an exhibit called <i>Homage to Surrealism</i>, celebrating the fortieth anniversary of Surrealism, which contained works by Dalí, <a href="/wiki/Joan_Mir%C3%B3" title="Joan Miró">Joan Miró</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enrique_T%C3%A1bara" title="Enrique Tábara">Enrique Tábara</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eugenio_Granell" title="Eugenio Granell">Eugenio Granell</a>. Breton vehemently fought against the inclusion of Dalí's <i>Sistine Madonna</i> in the International Surrealism Exhibition in New York the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-lopez_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lopez-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Late in his career Dalí did not confine himself to painting, but explored many unusual or novel media and processes: for example, he experimented with <a href="/wiki/Bulletism" title="Bulletism">bulletist</a> artworks.<sup id="cite_ref-bp_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bp-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Many of his late works incorporated <a href="/wiki/Optical_illusion" title="Optical illusion">optical illusions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Negative_space" title="Negative space">negative space</a>, <a href="/wiki/Visual_pun" title="Visual pun">visual puns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trompe_l%27%C5%93il" class="mw-redirect" title="Trompe l&#39;œil">trompe l'œil</a> visual effects. He also experimented with <a href="/wiki/Pointillism" title="Pointillism">pointillism</a>, enlarged <a href="/wiki/Half-tone" class="mw-redirect" title="Half-tone">half-tone</a> dot grids (a technique which <a href="/wiki/Roy_Lichtenstein" title="Roy Lichtenstein">Roy Lichtenstein</a> would later use), and <a href="/wiki/Stereoscopic" class="mw-redirect" title="Stereoscopic">stereoscopic</a> images.<sup id="cite_ref-AdesOptical_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AdesOptical-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> He was among the first artists to employ <a href="/wiki/Holography" title="Holography">holography</a> in an artistic manner.<sup id="cite_ref-holo_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-holo-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> In Dalí's later years, young artists such as <a href="/wiki/Andy_Warhol" title="Andy Warhol">Andy Warhol</a> proclaimed him an important influence on <a href="/wiki/Pop_art" title="Pop art">pop art</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-warhol_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-warhol-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dalí also developed a keen interest in natural science and <a href="/wiki/Mathematics_and_art" title="Mathematics and art">mathematics</a>. This is manifested in several of his paintings, notably from the 1950s, in which he painted his subjects as composed of <a href="/wiki/Rhinoceros_horn" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhinoceros horn">rhinoceros horn</a> shapes. According to Dalí, the rhinoceros horn signifies divine geometry because it grows in a <a href="/wiki/Logarithmic_spiral" title="Logarithmic spiral">logarithmic spiral</a>. He linked the rhinoceros to themes of <a href="/wiki/Chastity" title="Chastity">chastity</a> and to the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Dalí was also fascinated by <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tesseract" title="Tesseract">tesseract</a> (a four-dimensional cube); an unfolding of a hypercube is featured in the painting <i><a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_(Corpus_Hypercubus)" title="Crucifixion (Corpus Hypercubus)">Crucifixion (Corpus Hypercubus)</a></i>.
</p><p>At some point, Dalí had a <a href="/wiki/Glass_floor" title="Glass floor">glass floor</a> installed in a room near his studio in Lligat. He made extensive use of it to study <a href="/wiki/Foreshortening" class="mw-redirect" title="Foreshortening">foreshortening</a>, both from above and from below, incorporating dramatic perspectives of figures and objects into his paintings.<sup id="cite_ref-AdesOptical_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AdesOptical-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>17–18, 172</span></sup> He also delighted in using the room for entertaining guests and visitors to his house and studio. In many of his paintings, Dalí used <a href="/wiki/Anamorphosis" title="Anamorphosis">anamorphosis</a>, a form of eccentric and exaggerated perspective which distorts objects beyond recognition; however, when seen from a particular skewed viewpoint, a legible depiction emerges. He used the power of this technique to conceal "secret" or "forbidden" images in plain sight.<sup id="cite_ref-AdesOptical_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AdesOptical-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>20–25</span></sup>
</p><p>Dalí's post-World War II period bore the hallmarks of technical virtuosity and an intensifying interest in optical effects, science, and religion. He became an increasingly devout Catholic, while at the same time he had been inspired by the shock of <a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a> and the dawning of the "<a href="/wiki/Atomic_age" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic age">atomic age</a>". Therefore, Dalí labeled this period "Nuclear <a href="/wiki/Christian_mysticism" title="Christian mysticism">Mysticism</a>". In paintings such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Madonna_of_Port_Lligat" title="The Madonna of Port Lligat">The Madonna of Port Lligat</a></i> (first version, 1949) and <i>Corpus Hypercubus</i> (1954), Dalí sought to synthesize Christian <a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">iconography</a> with images of material disintegration inspired by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_physics" title="Nuclear physics">nuclear physics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> His Nuclear Mysticism works included such notable pieces as <i><a href="/wiki/La_Gare_de_Perpignan" title="La Gare de Perpignan">La Gare de Perpignan</a></i> (1965) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hallucinogenic_Toreador" title="The Hallucinogenic Toreador">The Hallucinogenic Toreador</a></i> (1968–70).
</p><p>In 1960, Dalí began work on his <a href="/wiki/Dal%C3%AD_Theatre_and_Museum" title="Dalí Theatre and Museum">Theatre and Museum</a> in his home town of <a href="/wiki/Figueres" title="Figueres">Figueres</a>; it was his largest single project and a main focus of his energy through 1974, when it opened. He continued to make additions through the mid-1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-Pitxot_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pitxot-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FGSD_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FGSD-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dalí continued to indulge in <a href="/wiki/Publicity_stunt" title="Publicity stunt">publicity stunts</a> and self-consciously outrageous behavior. To promote his 1962 book <i>The World of Salvador Dalí</i>, he appeared in a Manhattan bookstore on a bed, wired up to a machine that traced his <a href="/wiki/Electroencephalography" title="Electroencephalography">brain waves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a>. He would autograph books while thus monitored, and the book buyer would also be given the paper chart recording.<sup id="cite_ref-Meisler_6-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meisler-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1968, Dalí filmed a humorous television advertisement for <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lanvin_(chocolate)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Lanvin (chocolate) (page does not exist)">Lanvin</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lanvin_(chocolat)" class="extiw" title="fr:Lanvin (chocolat)">fr</a>&#93;</span> chocolates.<sup id="cite_ref-Augustin_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Augustin-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> In this, he proclaims in French "Je suis fou du chocolat Lanvin!" ("I'm crazy about Lanvin chocolate!") while biting a morsel, causing him to become <a href="/wiki/Strabismus" title="Strabismus">cross-eyed</a> and his moustache to swivel upwards.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>  Also in 1968, his status as an extravagant artist was put to use in a publicity campaign ("If you got it, flaunt it!") for <a href="/wiki/Braniff_International_Airlines" class="mw-redirect" title="Braniff International Airlines">Braniff International Airlines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Namath_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Namath-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1969, he designed the <a href="/wiki/Chupa_Chups" title="Chupa Chups">Chupa Chups</a> logo,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> in addition to facilitating the design of the advertising campaign for the <a href="/wiki/1969_Eurovision_Song_Contest" class="mw-redirect" title="1969 Eurovision Song Contest">1969 Eurovision Song Contest</a> and creating a large on-stage metal sculpture that stood at the <a href="/wiki/Teatro_Real" title="Teatro Real">Teatro Real</a> in Madrid.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the television programme <i>Dirty Dalí: A Private View</i> broadcast on <a href="/wiki/Channel_4" title="Channel 4">Channel 4</a> on 3 June 2007, art critic <a href="/wiki/Brian_Sewell" title="Brian Sewell">Brian Sewell</a> described his acquaintance with Dalí in the late 1960s, which included lying down in the fetal position without trousers in the armpit of a figure of Christ and masturbating for Dalí, who pretended to take photos while fumbling in his own trousers.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years_and_death">Final years and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Final years and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:20061227-Figueres_Sant_Pere_MQ.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/20061227-Figueres_Sant_Pere_MQ.jpg/220px-20061227-Figueres_Sant_Pere_MQ.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/20061227-Figueres_Sant_Pere_MQ.jpg/330px-20061227-Figueres_Sant_Pere_MQ.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/20061227-Figueres_Sant_Pere_MQ.jpg/440px-20061227-Figueres_Sant_Pere_MQ.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:20061227-Figueres_Sant_Pere_MQ.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Church of <i>Sant Pere</i> in <a href="/wiki/Figueres" title="Figueres">Figueres</a>, site of Dalí's baptism, first communion, and funeral</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Salvador_Dali_Crypt_in_Figueres.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Salvador_Dali_Crypt_in_Figueres.jpg/220px-Salvador_Dali_Crypt_in_Figueres.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Salvador_Dali_Crypt_in_Figueres.jpg/330px-Salvador_Dali_Crypt_in_Figueres.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Salvador_Dali_Crypt_in_Figueres.jpg/440px-Salvador_Dali_Crypt_in_Figueres.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Salvador_Dali_Crypt_in_Figueres.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dalí's crypt at the <a href="/wiki/Dal%C3%AD_Theatre_and_Museum" title="Dalí Theatre and Museum">Dalí Theatre and Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Figueres" title="Figueres">Figueres</a> displays his name and preferred title</div></div></div>
<p>In 1968, Dalí had bought a <a href="/wiki/Castle_of_P%C3%BAbol" title="Castle of Púbol">castle in Púbol</a> for Gala; and starting in 1971 she would retreat there alone for weeks at a time. By Dalí's own admission, he had agreed not to go there without written permission from his wife.<sup id="cite_ref-GalaGSDF_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GalaGSDF-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> His fears of abandonment and estrangement from his longtime artistic muse contributed to depression and failing health.<sup id="cite_ref-Meisler_6-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meisler-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1980 at age 76, Dalí's health took a catastrophic turn. His right hand trembled terribly, with <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson&#39;s disease">Parkinson-like</a> symptoms. His near-<a href="/wiki/Senile" class="mw-redirect" title="Senile">senile</a> wife allegedly had been dosing him with a dangerous cocktail of unprescribed medicine that damaged his nervous system, thus causing an untimely end to his artistic capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-Gibson,_Ian_1997_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gibson,_Ian_1997-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1982, <a href="/wiki/Juan_Carlos_I_of_Spain" title="Juan Carlos I of Spain">King Juan Carlos</a> bestowed on Dalí the title of <i>Marqués de Dalí de Púbol</i><sup id="cite_ref-Marquess_title_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marquess_title-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> (<i>Marquis of Dalí de Púbol</i>) in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_nobility" title="Spanish nobility">nobility of Spain</a>, hereby referring to <a href="/wiki/P%C3%BAbol" title="Púbol">Púbol</a>, the place where he lived. The title was in first instance hereditary, but on request of Dalí changed to life only in 1983.<sup id="cite_ref-Marquess_title_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marquess_title-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gala died on 10 June 1982, at the age of 87. After Gala's death, Dalí lost much of his will to live. He deliberately <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydrated</a> himself, possibly as a suicide attempt; there are also claims that he had tried to put himself into a state of <a href="/wiki/Suspended_animation" title="Suspended animation">suspended animation</a> as he had read that some <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> could do.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> He moved from Figueres to <a href="/wiki/Castle_of_P%C3%BAbol" title="Castle of Púbol">the castle in Púbol</a>, which was the site of her death and her grave.<sup id="cite_ref-Meisler_6-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meisler-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GalaGSDF_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GalaGSDF-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 1983, Dalí revealed what would be his last painting, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Swallow%27s_Tail" title="The Swallow&#39;s Tail">The Swallow's Tail</a></i>, a work heavily influenced by the mathematical <a href="/wiki/Catastrophe_theory" title="Catastrophe theory">catastrophe theory</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Thom" title="René Thom">René Thom</a>.
</p><p>In 1984, a fire broke out in his bedroom<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> under unclear circumstances. It was possibly a suicide attempt by Dalí, or possibly simple negligence by his staff. Dalí was rescued by friend and collaborator <a href="/wiki/Robert_Descharnes" title="Robert Descharnes">Robert Descharnes</a><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> and returned to Figueres, where a group of his friends, patrons, and fellow artists saw to it that he was comfortable living in his <a href="/wiki/Dal%C3%AD_Theatre_and_Museum" title="Dalí Theatre and Museum">Theater-Museum</a> in his final years.
</p><p>There have been allegations that Dalí was forced by his guardians to sign blank canvases that would later, even after his death, be used in forgeries and sold as originals.<sup id="cite_ref-scandal_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scandal-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> It is also alleged that he knowingly sold otherwise-blank lithograph paper which he had signed, possibly producing over 50,000 such sheets from 1965 until his death.<sup id="cite_ref-Meisler_6-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meisler-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> As a result, art dealers tend to be wary of late graphic works attributed to Dalí.<sup id="cite_ref-Forde_170_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forde_170-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 1988, Dalí entered the hospital with <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">heart failure</a>; a <a href="/wiki/Pacemaker" class="mw-redirect" title="Pacemaker">pacemaker</a> had been implanted previously. On 5 December 1988, he was visited by King Juan Carlos, who confessed that he had always been a serious devotee of Dalí.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Dalí gave the king a drawing, <i>Head of Europa</i>, which would turn out to be Dalí's final drawing.
</p><p>In early January 1989, Dali was returned to the Teatro-Museo and on his return he made his last public appearance. He was taken in a wheelchair to a room where press and TV were waiting and made a brief statement, saying:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>When you are a genius, you do not have the right to die, because we are necessary for the progress of humanity.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>On the morning of 23 January 1989, while his favorite record of <i><a href="/wiki/Tristan_und_Isolde" title="Tristan und Isolde">Tristan and Isolde</a></i> played, Dalí died of heart failure at the age of 84. He is buried in the <a href="/wiki/Crypt" title="Crypt">crypt</a> below the stage of his <a href="/wiki/Dal%C3%AD_Theatre_and_Museum" title="Dalí Theatre and Museum">Theatre and Museum</a> in Figueres. The location is across the street from the church of <i>Sant Pere</i>, where he had his <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a>, <a href="/wiki/First_communion" class="mw-redirect" title="First communion">first communion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Funeral" title="Funeral">funeral</a>, and is only 450 metres (1,480&#160;ft) from the house where he was born.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gala-Salvador_Dal%C3%AD_Foundation" title="Gala-Salvador Dalí Foundation">The Gala-Salvador Dalí Foundation</a> currently serves as his official estate.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The US <a href="/wiki/Copyright" title="Copyright">copyright</a> representative for the Gala-Salvador Dalí Foundation is the <a href="/wiki/Artists_Rights_Society" title="Artists Rights Society">Artists Rights Society</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Exhumation">Exhumation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Exhumation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On 26 June 2017 it was announced that a judge in Madrid had ordered the exhumation of Dali's body in order to obtain samples for a <a href="/wiki/Paternity_suit" class="mw-redirect" title="Paternity suit">paternity suit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Maria Pilar Abel Martínez, who works as a psychic and <a href="/wiki/Tarot" title="Tarot">tarot</a> card reader<sup id="cite_ref-Radford_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Radford-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> from <a href="/wiki/Figueres" title="Figueres">Figueres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Girona" title="Girona">Girona</a>, born in 1956, had stated that her mother, a maid, had been having an affair with the painter in 1955. Ms Abel claimed that her mother had told her that Dalí was her father. At the time of the alleged affair, Dalí was married to Gala.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The exhumation took place on the evening of 20 July, and DNA was extracted.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>  On Wednesday 6 September 2017 the Dali Foundation stated that the tests carried out proved conclusively that Dali and Martinez were not related.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Josep_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Josep-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Joan Manuel Sevillano, manager of the <i>Fundación Gala Salvador Dalí</i>, denounced the exhumation as inappropriate.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbolism">Symbolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Symbolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Dalí employed extensive symbolism in his work. For instance, the hallmark "melting watches" that first appear in <i>The Persistence of Memory</i> suggest <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein</a>'s theory that <a href="/wiki/Special_Relativity" class="mw-redirect" title="Special Relativity">time is relative</a> and not fixed.<sup id="cite_ref-Conquete_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conquete-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The idea for clocks functioning symbolically in this way came to Dalí when he was staring at a runny piece of <a href="/wiki/Camembert_(cheese)" class="mw-redirect" title="Camembert (cheese)">Camembert cheese</a> on a hot August day.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Elephant" title="Elephant">elephant</a> is also a recurring image in Dalí's works. It appeared in his 1944 work <i><a href="/wiki/Dream_Caused_by_the_Flight_of_a_Bee_Around_a_Pomegranate_a_Second_Before_Awakening" title="Dream Caused by the Flight of a Bee Around a Pomegranate a Second Before Awakening">Dream Caused by the Flight of a Bee Around a Pomegranate a Second Before Awakening</a></i>. The elephants, inspired by <a href="/wiki/Gian_Lorenzo_Bernini" title="Gian Lorenzo Bernini">Gian Lorenzo Bernini</a>'s sculpture <a href="/wiki/Obelisks_in_Rome#6" class="mw-redirect" title="Obelisks in Rome">base</a> in Rome of an <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_sopra_Minerva#Minerva&#39;s_Pulcino" title="Santa Maria sopra Minerva">elephant carrying an ancient obelisk</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> are portrayed "with long, multijointed, almost invisible legs of desire"<sup id="cite_ref-countycollection_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-countycollection-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> along with obelisks on their backs. Coupled with the image of their brittle legs, these encumbrances, noted for their phallic overtones, create a sense of phantom reality. "The elephant is a distortion in space", one analysis explains, "its spindly legs contrasting the idea of weightlessness with structure."<sup id="cite_ref-countycollection_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-countycollection-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> "I am painting pictures which make me die for joy, I am creating with an absolute naturalness, without the slightest aesthetic concern, I am making things that inspire me with a profound emotion and I am trying to paint them honestly."&#160;– Salvador Dalí, in Dawn Ades, <i>Dalí and Surrealism</i>.
</p><p>The egg is another common Dalíesque image. He connects the egg to the prenatal and intrauterine, thus using it to symbolize hope and love;<sup id="cite_ref-symb_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-symb-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> it appears in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Masturbator" title="The Great Masturbator">The Great Masturbator</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Metamorphosis_of_Narcissus" title="Metamorphosis of Narcissus">The Metamorphosis of Narcissus</a></i>. <i>The Metamorphosis of Narcissus</i> also symbolized death and <a href="/wiki/Petrification" class="mw-redirect" title="Petrification">petrification</a>. There are also giant sculptures of eggs in various locations at Dalí's house in Port Lligat<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> as well as at the <a href="/wiki/Dal%C3%AD_Theatre_and_Museum" title="Dalí Theatre and Museum">Dalí Theatre and Museum</a> in Figueres.
</p><p>Various other animals appear throughout his work as well: <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ants</a> point to death, decay, and immense sexual desire; the <a href="/wiki/Snail" title="Snail">snail</a> is connected to the human head (he saw a snail on a bicycle outside Freud's house when he first met <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a>); and <a href="/wiki/Locust" title="Locust">locusts</a> are a symbol of waste and fear.<sup id="cite_ref-symb_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-symb-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Both Dalí and his father enjoyed eating <a href="/wiki/Sea_urchin" title="Sea urchin">sea urchins</a>, freshly caught in the sea near Cadaqués. The <a href="/wiki/Radial_symmetry" class="mw-redirect" title="Radial symmetry">radial symmetry</a> of the sea urchin fascinated Dalí, and he adapted its form to many art works. Other foods also appear throughout his work.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science">Science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>References to Dalí in the context of science are made in terms of his fascination with the paradigm shift that accompanied the birth of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> in the twentieth century. Inspired by <a href="/wiki/Werner_Heisenberg" title="Werner Heisenberg">Werner Heisenberg</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Uncertainty_principle" title="Uncertainty principle">uncertainty principle</a>, in 1958 he wrote in his "Anti-Matter Manifesto": "In the Surrealist period, I wanted to create the iconography of the interior world and the world of the marvelous, of my father <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Freud</a>. Today, the exterior world and that of physics has transcended the one of psychology. My father today is Dr. Heisenberg."<sup id="cite_ref-triangle_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-triangle-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In this respect, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Disintegration_of_the_Persistence_of_Memory" title="The Disintegration of the Persistence of Memory">The Disintegration of the Persistence of Memory</a></i>, which appeared in 1954, in harking back to <i>The Persistence of Memory</i> and in portraying that painting in fragmentation and disintegration, summarizes Dalí's acknowledgment of the new science.<sup id="cite_ref-triangle_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-triangle-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Endeavors_outside_painting">Endeavors outside painting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Endeavors outside painting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Dalí was a versatile artist. Some of his more popular works are sculptures and other objects, and he is also noted for his contributions to theatre, fashion, and photography, among other areas.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sculptures_and_other_objects">Sculptures and other objects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Sculptures and other objects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hommage_%C3%A0_Newton.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Hommage_%C3%A0_Newton.jpg/170px-Hommage_%C3%A0_Newton.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Hommage_%C3%A0_Newton.jpg/255px-Hommage_%C3%A0_Newton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Hommage_%C3%A0_Newton.jpg/340px-Hommage_%C3%A0_Newton.jpg 2x" data-file-width="521" data-file-height="721" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hommage_%C3%A0_Newton.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Homage to Newton</i> (1985), Bronze with dark patina. <a href="/wiki/UOB_Plaza" title="UOB Plaza">UOB Plaza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>. Dalí's homage to <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>, with an open torso and suspended heart to indicate "open-heartedness," and an open head indicating "open-mindedness"<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></div></div></div>
<p>Two of the most popular objects of the surrealist movement were <i><a href="/wiki/Lobster_Telephone" title="Lobster Telephone">Lobster Telephone</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Mae_West_Lips_Sofa" title="Mae West Lips Sofa">Mae West Lips Sofa</a></i>, completed by Dalí in 1936 and 1937, respectively.
