Surrealist artist and patron <a href="/wiki/Edward_James" title="Edward James">Edward James</a> commissioned both of these pieces from Dalí; James inherited a large English estate in <a href="/wiki/West_Dean,_West_Sussex" title="West Dean, West Sussex">West Dean</a>, West Sussex when he was five and was one of the foremost supporters of the surrealists in the 1930s.<sup id="cite_ref-natgalaust_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natgalaust-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> "Lobsters and telephones had strong sexual connotations for [Dalí]", according to the display caption for the <i>Lobster Telephone</i> at the <a href="/wiki/Tate_Gallery" class="mw-redirect" title="Tate Gallery">Tate Gallery</a>, "and he drew a close analogy between food and sex."<sup id="cite_ref-tate_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tate-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> The telephone was functional, and James purchased four of them from Dalí to replace the phones in his retreat home. One now appears at the Tate Gallery; the second can be found at the German Telephone Museum in <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a>; the third belongs to the Edward James Foundation; and the fourth is at the <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery_of_Australia" title="National Gallery of Australia">National Gallery of Australia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-natgalaust_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natgalaust-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The wood and satin <i>Mae West Lips Sofa</i> was shaped after the lips of actress <a href="/wiki/Mae_West" title="Mae West">Mae West</a>, whom Dalí apparently found fascinating.<sup id="cite_ref-unbound_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unbound-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> West was previously the subject of Dalí's 1935 painting, <i>The Face of Mae West which may be used as an apartment</i>. The <i>Mae West Lips Sofa</i> currently resides at the Museum Boijmans Van Beuningen in Rotterdam. Another version is on display at the Dalí Theatre and Museum in Figueres, Catalonia, Spain.
</p><p>Between 1941 and 1970, Dalí created an ensemble of 39 pieces of jewelry; many pieces are intricate, and some contain moving parts. The most famous assemblage, <i>The Royal Heart</i>, is made of gold and is encrusted with 46 rubies, 42 diamonds, and four emeralds, created in such a way that the center "beats" much like a real heart. Dalí himself commented that "Without an audience, without the presence of spectators, these jewels would not fulfill the function for which they came into being. The viewer, then, is the ultimate artist."<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> The "Dalí – Joies" ("The Jewels of Dalí") collection is in the Dalí Theater Museum in Figueres, Catalonia, Spain.
</p><p>Dalí took a stab at <a href="/wiki/Industrial_design" title="Industrial design">industrial design</a> in the 1970s with a 500-piece run of the upscale <i>Suomi</i> tableware by <a href="/wiki/Timo_Sarpaneva" title="Timo Sarpaneva">Timo Sarpaneva</a> that Dalí decorated for the German Rosenthal <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a> maker's "Studio Linie".<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dali_Sundial_in_Paris.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Dali_Sundial_in_Paris.jpg/220px-Dali_Sundial_in_Paris.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Dali_Sundial_in_Paris.jpg/330px-Dali_Sundial_in_Paris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Dali_Sundial_in_Paris.jpg/440px-Dali_Sundial_in_Paris.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="894" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dali_Sundial_in_Paris.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sundial painted by Dali, 27 <a href="/wiki/Rue_Saint-Jacques,_Paris" title="Rue Saint-Jacques, Paris">Rue Saint-Jacques, Paris</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theatre_and_film">Theatre and film</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Theatre and film">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In theatre, Dalí constructed the scenery for <a href="/wiki/Federico_Garc%C3%ADa_Lorca" title="Federico García Lorca">Federico García Lorca</a>'s 1927 romantic play <i><a href="/wiki/Mariana_Pineda" title="Mariana Pineda">Mariana Pineda</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> For <i><a href="/wiki/Bacchanale" title="Bacchanale">Bacchanale</a></i> (1939), a ballet based on and set to the music of <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a>'s 1845 opera <i><a href="/wiki/Tannh%C3%A4user_(opera)" title="Tannhäuser (opera)">Tannhäuser</a></i>, Dalí provided both the set design and the <a href="/wiki/Libretto" title="Libretto">libretto</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-designws_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-designws-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> <i>Bacchanale</i> was followed by set designs for <i>Labyrinth</i> in 1941 and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Three-Cornered_Hat" title="The Three-Cornered Hat">The Three-Cornered Hat</a></i> in 1949.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dalí became intensely interested in film when he was young, going to the theatre most Sundays. He was part of the era where silent films were being viewed and drawing on the medium of film became popular. He believed there were two dimensions to the theories of film and cinema: "things themselves", the facts that are presented in the world of the camera; and "photographic imagination", the way the camera shows the picture and how creative or imaginative it looks.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Dalí was active in front of and behind the scenes in the film world.
</p><p>He is credited as co-creator of <a href="/wiki/Luis_Bu%C3%B1uel" title="Luis Buñuel">Luis Buñuel</a>'s surrealist film <i><a href="/wiki/Un_Chien_Andalou" title="Un Chien Andalou">Un Chien Andalou</a></i>, a 17-minute French art film co-written with Luis Buñuel that is widely remembered for its graphic opening scene simulating the slashing of a human eyeball with a razor. In <i>Un Chien Andalou</i>, surreal imagery and irrational discontinuities in time and space produce a dreamlike quality.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The second film he produced with Buñuel was entitled <i><a href="/wiki/L%27Age_d%27Or" title="L&#39;Age d&#39;Or">L'Age d'Or</a></i>, and it was performed at Studio 28 in Paris in 1930. <i>L'Age d'Or</i> was "banned for years after fascist and anti-Semitic groups staged a stink bomb and ink-throwing riot in the Paris theater where it was shown".<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Both of these films, <i>Un Chien Andalou</i> and <i>L'Age d'Or</i>, have had a tremendous impact on the independent surrealist film movement. "If <i>Un Chien Andalou</i> stands as the supreme record of Surrealism's adventures into the realm of the unconscious, then <i>L'Âge d'Or</i> is perhaps the most trenchant and implacable expression of its revolutionary intent".<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dalí worked with other famous filmmakers, such as <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Hitchcock" title="Alfred Hitchcock">Alfred Hitchcock</a>. The most well-known of his film projects is probably the <a href="/wiki/Dream_sequence" title="Dream sequence">dream sequence</a> in Hitchcock's <i><a href="/wiki/Spellbound_(1945_film)" title="Spellbound (1945 film)">Spellbound</a></i>, which delves into themes of <a href="/wiki/Psychoanalysis" title="Psychoanalysis">psychoanalysis</a>. Hitchcock needed a dreamlike quality to his film, which dealt with the idea that a repressed experience can directly trigger a <a href="/wiki/Neurosis" title="Neurosis">neurosis</a>, and he knew that Dalí's work would help create the atmosphere he wanted in his film.
</p><p>Dalí also worked with <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a> on the short film production <i><a href="/wiki/Destino" title="Destino">Destino</a></i>. Completed in 2003 by Baker Bloodworth and Walt's nephew <a href="/wiki/Roy_E._Disney" title="Roy E. Disney">Roy E. Disney</a>, it contains dreamlike images of strange figures flying and walking about. It is based on Mexican songwriter Armando Dominguez' song "Destino". When Disney hired Dalí to help produce the film in 1946, they were not prepared for the quantity of work that lay ahead. For eight months, they worked on it continuously, until their efforts had to stop when they realized they were in financial trouble. However, it was eventually finished 48 years later, and shown in various film festivals. The film consists of Dalí's artwork interacting with Disney's character animation.
</p><p>In 1960 Dalí and the photographer <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Halsman" title="Philippe Halsman">Philippe Halsman</a> made a documentary video called <i>Chaos and Creation</i>, that showed him creating a painting.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dalí completed only one other film in his lifetime, <i><a href="/wiki/Impressions_de_la_Haute_Mongolie" title="Impressions de la Haute Mongolie">Impressions of Upper Mongolia</a></i> (1975), in which he narrated a story about an expedition in search of giant <a href="/wiki/Psilocybin_mushroom" title="Psilocybin mushroom">hallucinogenic mushrooms</a>. The imagery was based on microscopic <a href="/wiki/Uric_acid" title="Uric acid">uric acid</a> stains on the brass band of a ballpoint pen on which Dalí had been urinating for several weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the mid-1970s, film director <a href="/wiki/Alejandro_Jodorowsky" title="Alejandro Jodorowsky">Alejandro Jodorowsky</a> cast Dali in the role of the Padishah Emperor in a production of <i><a href="/wiki/Dune_(novel)" title="Dune (novel)">Dune</a></i>, based on the novel by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Herbert" title="Frank Herbert">Frank Herbert</a>. According to the 2013 documentary on the film, <i><a href="/wiki/Jodorowsky%27s_Dune" title="Jodorowsky&#39;s Dune">Jodorowsky's Dune</a></i>, Jodorowsky met Dali in the <a href="/wiki/St._Regis_New_York#King_Cole_Bar" title="St. Regis New York">King Cole Bar</a> in the <a href="/wiki/St._Regis_New_York" title="St. Regis New York">St. Regis hotel</a> in Manhattan to discuss the role. Dali expressed interest in the film but required as a condition of appearing that he be made the highest-paid actor in Hollywood. Jodorowsky accordingly cast Dali as the emperor, but he planned to cut Dali's screen time to mere minutes, promising he be the highest-paid actor on a per minute basis. The film was ultimately never made.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the year 1927, Dali began to write the libretto for an opera, which he called <i><a href="/wiki/%C3%8Atre_Dieu" title="Être Dieu">Être Dieu</a></i> (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140802151038/http://www.salvadorbrand.com/Salvador-Brand/opera.html"><i>To Be God</i></a>). He wrote this together with Federico Garcia Lorca one afternoon in the Café Regina Victoria in Madrid. In 1974, for a recording in Paris, the opera was adapted by the Spanish writer Manuel Vazquez Montalban, who wrote the libretto, while the music was created by Igor Wakhevitch. During the recording, however, Dali refused to follow the text written by Montalban, and instead, began to improvise in the belief that “Salvador Dali never repeats himself.”
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fashion_and_photography">Fashion and photography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Fashion and photography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Salvador_Dali_A_(Dali_Atomicus)_09633u.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Salvador_Dali_A_%28Dali_Atomicus%29_09633u.jpg/220px-Salvador_Dali_A_%28Dali_Atomicus%29_09633u.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Salvador_Dali_A_%28Dali_Atomicus%29_09633u.jpg/330px-Salvador_Dali_A_%28Dali_Atomicus%29_09633u.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Salvador_Dali_A_%28Dali_Atomicus%29_09633u.jpg/440px-Salvador_Dali_A_%28Dali_Atomicus%29_09633u.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3703" data-file-height="2971" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Salvador_Dali_A_(Dali_Atomicus)_09633u.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Dali Atomicus</i>, photo by <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Halsman" title="Philippe Halsman">Philippe Halsman</a> (1948), shown before support wires were removed from the image</div></div></div>
<p>Dalí built a repertoire in the <a href="/wiki/Fashion" title="Fashion">fashion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photography" title="Photography">photography</a> businesses as well. His cooperation with Italian fashion designer <a href="/wiki/Elsa_Schiaparelli" title="Elsa Schiaparelli">Elsa Schiaparelli</a> was well-known, when Dalí was commissioned to produce a white dress with a lobster print. Other designs Dalí made for her include a shoe-shaped hat, and a pink belt with lips for a buckle. He was also involved in creating textile designs and perfume bottles. In 1950, Dalí created a special "costume for the year 2045" with <a href="/wiki/Christian_Dior" title="Christian Dior">Christian Dior</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-designws_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-designws-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Photographers with whom he collaborated include <a href="/wiki/Man_Ray" title="Man Ray">Man Ray</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brassa%C3%AF" title="Brassaï">Brassaï</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Beaton" title="Cecil Beaton">Cecil Beaton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Halsman" title="Philippe Halsman">Philippe Halsman</a>. With Man Ray and Brassaï, Dalí photographed nature; with the others, he explored a range of obscure topics, including (with Halsman) the <i>Dalí Atomica</i> series (1948)&#160;–  inspired by his painting <i>Leda Atomica</i>&#160;&#160;–  which in one photograph depicts "a painter's easel, three cats, a bucket of water, and Dalí himself floating in the air."<sup id="cite_ref-designws_119-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-designws-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One of Dalí's most unorthodox artistic creations may have been an entire <a href="/wiki/Persona" title="Persona">persona</a>, in addition to his own. At a French <a href="/wiki/Nightclub" title="Nightclub">nightclub</a> in 1965, Dalí met <a href="/wiki/Amanda_Lear" title="Amanda Lear">Amanda Lear</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Fashion_model" class="mw-redirect" title="Fashion model">fashion model</a> then known as Peki D'Oslo.<sup id="cite_ref-Prose_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prose-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Lear became his protégée and muse,<sup id="cite_ref-Prose_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prose-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> later writing about their affair in her authorized biography <i>My Life With Dalí</i> (1986).<sup id="cite_ref-Lear_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lear-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Transfixed by the mannish, larger-than-life Lear, Dalí masterminded her successful transition from modeling to the music world, advising her on self-presentation and helping spin mysterious stories about her origin as she took the <a href="/wiki/Disco" title="Disco">disco</a>-art scene by storm. According to Lear, she and Dalí were united in a "spiritual marriage" on a deserted mountaintop.<sup id="cite_ref-Prose_128-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prose-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> She was referred to as Dalí's "Frankenstein",<sup id="cite_ref-Lozano_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lozano-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> and some observers believed Lear's assumed name was a <a href="/wiki/Pun" title="Pun">pun</a> on the French phrase "L'Amant Dalí", or "Lover of Dalí". Lear took the place of an earlier muse, <a href="/wiki/Ultra_Violet_(Isabelle_Collin_Dufresne)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ultra Violet (Isabelle Collin Dufresne)">Ultra Violet (Isabelle Collin Dufresne)</a>, who had left Dalí's side to join <a href="/wiki/The_Factory" title="The Factory">The Factory</a> of <a href="/wiki/Andy_Warhol" title="Andy Warhol">Andy Warhol</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Etherington-Smith_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Etherington-Smith-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Both former apprentices would go on to successfully promote their own careers in the arts. On April 10, 2005, they joined a panel discussion "Reminiscences of Dalí: A Conversation with Friends of the Artist" as part of a symposium "The Dalí Renaissance" for a major retrospective Dalí show at the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Museum_of_Art" title="Philadelphia Museum of Art">Philadelphia Museum of Art</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PMADali_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PMADali-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Their conversation is recorded in the 236-page exhibition catalog <i>The Dalí Renaissance: New Perspectives on His Life and Art after 1940</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dali_museum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Dali_museum.jpg/220px-Dali_museum.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Dali_museum.jpg/330px-Dali_museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/89/Dali_museum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="306" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dali_museum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dal%C3%AD_Theatre_and_Museum" title="Dalí Theatre and Museum">Dalí Theatre and Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Figueres" title="Figueres">Figueres</a> also holds the crypt where Dalí is buried</div></div></div>
<p>Architectural achievements include his <a href="/wiki/Port_Lligat" class="mw-redirect" title="Port Lligat">Port Lligat</a> house near <a href="/wiki/Cadaqu%C3%A9s" title="Cadaqués">Cadaqués</a>, as well as his <a href="/wiki/Dal%C3%AD_Theatre_and_Museum" title="Dalí Theatre and Museum">Theatre and Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Figueres" title="Figueres">Figueres</a>. A major work outside of Spain was the temporary <i>Dream of Venus</i> surrealist pavilion at the <a href="/wiki/1939_New_York_World%27s_Fair" title="1939 New York World&#39;s Fair">1939 New York World's Fair</a>, which contained within it a number of unusual sculptures and statues, including live performers posing as statues.<sup id="cite_ref-DrmVns_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DrmVns-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_works">Literary works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Literary works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Under the encouragement of poet <a href="/wiki/Federico_Garc%C3%ADa_Lorca" title="Federico García Lorca">Federico García Lorca</a>, Dalí attempted an approach to a literary career through the means of the "pure novel". In his only novel <i>Hidden Faces</i> (1944), Dalí describes, in vividly visual terms, the intrigues and love affairs of a group of dazzling, eccentric aristocrats who, with their luxurious and extravagant lifestyle, symbolize the decadence of the 1930s. The Comte de Grandsailles and Solange de Cléda pursue an awkward love affair, but property transactions, interwar political turmoil, the <a href="/wiki/French_Resistance" title="French Resistance">French Resistance</a>, his marriage to another woman and her responsibilities as a landowner and businesswoman drive them apart. It is variously set in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, rural France, <a href="/wiki/Casablanca" title="Casablanca">Casablanca</a> in North Africa and <a href="/wiki/Palm_Springs" class="mw-redirect" title="Palm Springs">Palm Springs</a> in the United States. Secondary characters include aging widow Barbara Rogers, her bisexual daughter Veronica, Veronica's sometime female lover Betka, and Baba, a disfigured US fighter pilot. The novel concludes at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, with Solange dying before Grandsailles can return to his former property and reunite with her.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> The novel was written in New York, and translated by <a href="/wiki/Haakon_Chevalier" title="Haakon Chevalier">Haakon Chevalier</a>.
</p><p>His other, nonfictional literary works include <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_Life_of_Salvador_Dal%C3%AD" title="The Secret Life of Salvador Dalí">The Secret Life of Salvador Dalí</a></i> (1942), <i>Diary of a Genius</i> (1952–63), and <i>Oui: The Paranoid-Critical Revolution</i> (1927–33).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Graphic_arts">Graphic arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Graphic arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The artist worked extensively in the <a href="/wiki/Graphic_arts" title="Graphic arts">graphic arts</a>, producing many etchings and <a href="/wiki/Lithograph" class="mw-redirect" title="Lithograph">lithographs</a>. While his early work in printmaking is equal in quality to his important paintings, as he grew older he would sell the rights to images but not be involved in the print production itself. In addition, a large number of fakes were produced in the 1980s and 1990s, thus further confusing the Dalí print market.<sup id="cite_ref-Forde_170_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forde_170-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics_and_personality">Politics and personality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Politics and personality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Salvador_Dali_NYWTS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Salvador_Dali_NYWTS.jpg/170px-Salvador_Dali_NYWTS.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Salvador_Dali_NYWTS.jpg/255px-Salvador_Dali_NYWTS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Salvador_Dali_NYWTS.jpg/340px-Salvador_Dali_NYWTS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3140" data-file-height="4096" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Salvador_Dali_NYWTS.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dalí in the 1960s sporting his characteristic flamboyant <a href="/wiki/Moustache" title="Moustache">moustache</a>. Photographed holding his pet <a href="/wiki/Ocelot" title="Ocelot">ocelot</a>, Babou.</div></div></div>
<p>Dalí's politics played a significant role in his emergence as an artist. In his youth, he embraced both <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">anarchism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> though his writings tell anecdotes of making radical political statements more to shock listeners than from any deep conviction. This was in keeping with Dalí's allegiance to the <a href="/wiki/Dada" title="Dada">Dada</a> movement.<sup id="cite_ref-Dali_Dada_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dali_Dada-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-navarro_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-navarro-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As he grew older his political allegiances changed, especially as the Surrealist movement went through transformations under the leadership of the <a href="/wiki/Trotsky" class="mw-redirect" title="Trotsky">Trotskyist</a> writer <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Breton" title="André Breton">André Breton</a>, who is said to have called Dalí in for questioning on his politics. In his 1970 book <i>Dalí by Dalí</i>, Dalí declared himself to be both an anarchist and <a href="/wiki/Monarchist" class="mw-redirect" title="Monarchist">monarchist</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a> (1936–1939), Dalí fled from the fighting and refused to align himself with any group. He did the same during World War II (1939–1945), for which he was heavily criticized; <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a> accused him of "scuttling off like a rat as soon as France is in danger" after Dalí had prospered in France during the pre-war years. "When the European War approaches he has one preoccupation only: how to find a place which has good cookery and from which he can make a quick bolt if danger comes too near", Orwell observed.<sup id="cite_ref-orwell_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-orwell-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> In a notable 1944 review of Dalí's autobiography, Orwell wrote, "One ought to be able to hold in one's head simultaneously the two facts that Dalí is a good draughtsman and a disgusting human being".<sup id="cite_ref-orwell_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-orwell-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After his return to Catalonia post World War II, Dalí moved closer to the <a href="/wiki/Authoritarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Authoritarian">authoritarian</a> regime of <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Francisco Franco</a>. Some of Dalí's statements were supportive, congratulating Franco for his actions aimed "at clearing Spain of destructive forces".<sup id="cite_ref-navarro_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-navarro-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Dalí, having returned to the Catholic faith and becoming increasingly religious as time went on, may have been referring to the <a href="/wiki/Second_Spanish_Republic" title="Second Spanish Republic">Republican</a> <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War#Atrocities" title="Spanish Civil War">atrocities during the Spanish Civil War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Dalí sent telegrams to Franco, praising him for signing <a href="/wiki/Death_warrant" class="mw-redirect" title="Death warrant">death warrants</a> for prisoners.<sup id="cite_ref-navarro_136-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-navarro-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> He even met Franco personally,<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> and painted a portrait of Franco's granddaughter.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He also once sent a telegram praising the <i>Conducător</i>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romanian</a> <a href="/wiki/Communist" class="mw-redirect" title="Communist">Communist</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Nicolae_Ceau%C5%9Fescu" class="mw-redirect" title="Nicolae Ceauşescu">Nicolae Ceauşescu</a>, for his adoption of a <a href="/wiki/Scepter" class="mw-redirect" title="Scepter">scepter</a> as part of his regalia. The Romanian daily newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/Sc%C3%AEnteia" title="Scînteia">Scînteia</a></i> published it, without suspecting its mocking aspect.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> One of Dalí's few possible bits of open disobedience was his continued praise of <a href="/wiki/Federico_Garc%C3%ADa_Lorca" title="Federico García Lorca">Federico García Lorca</a> even in the years when Lorca's works were banned.<sup id="cite_ref-conversations_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-conversations-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (August 2009)">failed verification</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Dalí, a colorful and imposing presence with his ever–present long cape, walking stick, haughty expression, and upturned waxed moustache, was famous for having said that "every morning upon awakening, I experience a supreme pleasure: that of being Salvador Dalí".<sup id="cite_ref-smithsonian_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smithsonian-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> In the 1960s, he gave the actress <a href="/wiki/Mia_Farrow" title="Mia Farrow">Mia Farrow</a> a dead mouse in a bottle, hand-painted, which her mother, actress <a href="/wiki/Maureen_O%27Sullivan" title="Maureen O&#39;Sullivan">Maureen O'Sullivan</a>, demanded be removed from her house.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dali's religious views were a matter of interest. In interviews Dali revealed his mysticism. In his later years, while still remaining a Roman Catholic, Dalí also claimed to be an agnostic.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> In his 1942 autobiography <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_Life_of_Salvador_Dali" class="mw-redirect" title="The Secret Life of Salvador Dali">The Secret Life of Salvador Dali</a></i>, he sums up his life story with an impassioned defense of the Catholic Church and religion in general. In one passage he states "I believe, above all, in the real and unfathomable force of the philosophic Catholicism of France and in that of the militant Catholicism of Spain."<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>  Dali also had great respect for the Jesuit priest and philosopher <a href="/wiki/Teilhard_de_Chardin" class="mw-redirect" title="Teilhard de Chardin">Teilhard de Chardin</a><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> and was fascinated by his <a href="/wiki/Omega_Point" title="Omega Point">Omega Point</a> theory (a theory wherein the universe evolves towards an ultimate state of complexity and spiritual consciousness).<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Dali's 1959 painting <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ecumenical_Council_(painting)" title="The Ecumenical Council (painting)">The Ecumenical Council</a></i> is said to represent the "interconnectedness" of the <a href="/wiki/Omega_Point" title="Omega Point">Omega Point</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dalí frequently traveled with his pet <a href="/wiki/Ocelot" title="Ocelot">ocelot</a> Babou, even bringing it aboard the luxury ocean liner <i><a href="/wiki/SS_France" title="SS France">SS France</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> He was also known to avoid paying tabs at restaurants by drawing on the checks he wrote. His theory was the restaurant would never want to cash such a valuable piece of art, and he was usually correct.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Besides visual puns, Dalí shared in the surrealist delight in verbal puns, obscure allusions, and word games. He often spoke in a bizarre combination of French, Spanish, Catalan, and English which was sometimes amusing as well as arcane.
</p><p>When interviewed by <a href="/wiki/Mike_Wallace" title="Mike Wallace">Mike Wallace</a> on his <i><a href="/wiki/60_Minutes" title="60 Minutes">60 Minutes</a></i> television show, Dalí kept referring to himself in the <a href="/wiki/Illeism" title="Illeism">third person</a>, as the "Divino Dalí" (Divine Dalí), and told the startled Wallace matter-of-factly that he did not believe in his death.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> On January 27, 1957, he was the mystery guest on the US panel show <i><a href="/wiki/What%27s_My_Line%3F" title="What&#39;s My Line?">What's My Line?</a></i> and signed the chalkboard with thick white paint.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> His answers were misleading and prompted guidance from host <a href="/wiki/John_Daly_(radio_and_television_personality)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Daly (radio and television personality)">John Daly</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dali appeared in public on a number of occasions with an <a href="/wiki/Anteater" title="Anteater">anteater</a>, notably on a lead in Paris in 1969 and on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dick_Cavett_Show" title="The Dick Cavett Show">The Dick Cavett Show</a></i> on March 6, 1970 when he carried a small anteater on-stage. On the show, he surprised fellow guest <a href="/wiki/Lillian_Gish" title="Lillian Gish">Lillian Gish</a> by flinging the anteater onto her lap.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In Carlos Lozano's biography, <i>Sex, Surrealism, Dalí, and Me</i>, produced with the collaboration of <a href="/wiki/Clifford_Thurlow" title="Clifford Thurlow">Clifford Thurlow</a>, Lozano makes it clear that Dalí never stopped being a surrealist. As Dalí said of himself: "the only difference between me and the surrealists is that I am a surrealist".<sup id="cite_ref-artcyclopedia_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-artcyclopedia-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Salvador Dalí has been cited as a major inspiration by many modern artists, such as <a href="/wiki/Damien_Hirst" title="Damien Hirst">Damien Hirst</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Koons" title="Jeff Koons">Jeff Koons</a>, and most other modern surrealists. Salvador Dalí's manic expression and famous moustache have made him something of a <a href="/wiki/Cultural_icon" title="Cultural icon">cultural icon</a> for the bizarre and surreal. He has been portrayed on film by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Pattinson" title="Robert Pattinson">Robert Pattinson</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Little_Ashes" title="Little Ashes">Little Ashes</a></i> (2008), and by <a href="/wiki/Adrien_Brody" title="Adrien Brody">Adrien Brody</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Midnight_in_Paris" title="Midnight in Paris">Midnight in Paris</a></i> (2011). He was also parodied in a series of painting skits on <i><a href="/wiki/Captain_Kangaroo" title="Captain Kangaroo">Captain Kangaroo</a></i> as "Salvador Silly" (played by Cosmo Allegretti) and in a <i><a href="/wiki/Sesame_Street" title="Sesame Street">Sesame Street</a></i> muppet skit as "Salvador Dada" (an orange gold Anything Muppet performed by <a href="/wiki/Jim_Henson" title="Jim Henson">Jim Henson</a>).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Salvador_Dal%C3%AD_Desert" title="Salvador Dalí Desert">Salvador Dalí Desert</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dali_(crater)" title="Dali (crater)">Dali crater</a> on the planet <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> are named for him.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_Arms_of_the_Marquess_of_Dal%C3%AD_de_P%C3%BAbol.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Coat_of_Arms_of_the_Marquess_of_Dal%C3%AD_de_P%C3%BAbol.svg/220px-Coat_of_Arms_of_the_Marquess_of_Dal%C3%AD_de_P%C3%BAbol.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="312" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Coat_of_Arms_of_the_Marquess_of_Dal%C3%AD_de_P%C3%BAbol.svg/330px-Coat_of_Arms_of_the_Marquess_of_Dal%C3%AD_de_P%C3%BAbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Coat_of_Arms_of_the_Marquess_of_Dal%C3%AD_de_P%C3%BAbol.svg/440px-Coat_of_Arms_of_the_Marquess_of_Dal%C3%AD_de_P%C3%BAbol.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="811" data-file-height="1149" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_Arms_of_the_Marquess_of_Dal%C3%AD_de_P%C3%BAbol.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Heraldry of the 1st Marquis of Dalí de Púbol</div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Honours">Honours</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Honours">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><b>1964</b>: Knight Grand Cross of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Isabella_the_Catholic" title="Order of Isabella the Catholic">Order of Isabella the Catholic</a><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>1972</b>: Associate member of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy_of_Science,_Letters_and_Fine_Arts_of_Belgium" title="Royal Academy of Science, Letters and Fine Arts of Belgium">Royal Academy of Science, Letters and Fine Arts of Belgium</a><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>1981</b>: Knight Grand Cross of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Charles_III" title="Order of Charles III">Order of Charles III</a><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>1982</b>: Created <i>1st Marqués de Dalí de Púbol</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Juan_Carlos_I_of_Spain" title="Juan Carlos I of Spain">King Juan Carlos</a></li>
<li>Member of the <a href="/wiki/Legion_of_Honour" title="Legion of Honour">Legion of Honour</a></li>
<li>Associate member of the <a href="/wiki/Acad%C3%A9mie_des_Beaux-Arts" title="Académie des Beaux-Arts">Académie des Beaux-Arts</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Institut_de_France" title="Institut de France">Institut de France</a></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_selected_works">List of selected works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: List of selected works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_works_by_Salvador_Dal%C3%AD" title="List of works by Salvador Dalí">List of works by Salvador Dalí</a></div>
<p>Dalí produced over 1,500 paintings in his career<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> in addition to producing illustrations for books, lithographs, designs for theatre sets and costumes, a great number of drawings, dozens of sculptures, and various other projects, including an animated short film for <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="The Walt Disney Company">Disney</a>. He also collaborated with director Jack Bond in 1965, creating a movie titled <i>Dalí in New York</i>. Below is a chronological sample of important and representative work, as well as some notes on what Dalí did in particular years.<sup id="cite_ref-Dali_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dali-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>1910 <i><a href="/wiki/Landscape_Near_Figueras" title="Landscape Near Figueras">Landscape Near Figueras</a></i></li>
<li>1913 <i><a href="/wiki/Vilabertran_(Dal%C3%AD)" title="Vilabertran (Dalí)">Vilabertin</a></i></li>
<li>1916 <i>Fiesta in Figueras</i> (begun 1914)</li>
<li>1917 <i>View of Cadaqués with Shadow of Mount Pani</i></li>
<li>1918 <i>Crepuscular Old Man</i> (begun 1917)</li>
<li>1919 <i>Port of Cadaqués (Night)</i> (begun 1918) and <i>Self-portrait in the Studio</i></li>
<li>1920 <i>The Artist's Father at Llane Beach</i> and <i>View of Portdogué (Port Aluger)</i></li>
<li>1921 <i>The Garden of Llaner (Cadaqués)</i> (begun 1920) and <i>Self-portrait</i></li>
<li>1922 <i><a href="/wiki/Cabaret_Scene" title="Cabaret Scene">Cabaret Scene</a></i> and <i>Night Walking Dreams</i></li>
<li>1923 <i>Self Portrait with L'Humanite</i> and <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cubist_Self_Portrait&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Cubist Self Portrait (page does not exist)">Cubist Self Portrait</a> with La Publicitat</i></li>
<li>1924 <i>Still Life (Syphon and Bottle of Rum)</i> (for <a href="/wiki/Garc%C3%ADa_Lorca" class="mw-redirect" title="García Lorca">García Lorca</a>) and <i>Portrait of Luis Buñuel</i></li>
<li>1925 <i>Large Harlequin and Small Bottle of Rum</i> and a series of fine portraits of his sister Anna Maria, most notably <i>Figure at a Window</i></li>
<li>1926 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Basket_of_Bread" title="The Basket of Bread">The Basket of Bread</a></i>, <i>Girl from Figueres</i> and <i>Girl with Curls</i></li>
<li>1927 <i>Composition with Three Figures (Neo-Cubist Academy)</i> and <i>Honey is Sweeter than Blood</i> (his first important surrealist work)</li>
<li>1929 <i lang="fr" title="French language text"><a href="/wiki/Un_Chien_Andalou" title="Un Chien Andalou">Un Chien Andalou</a></i> (<i>An Andalusian Dog</i>) film in collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Luis_Bu%C3%B1uel" title="Luis Buñuel">Luis Buñuel</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lugubrious_Game" title="The Lugubrious Game">The Lugubrious Game</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Masturbator" title="The Great Masturbator">The Great Masturbator</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_First_Days_of_Spring" title="The First Days of Spring">The First Days of Spring</a></i>, and <i>The Profanation of the Host</i></li>
<li>1930 <i lang="fr" title="French language text"><a href="/wiki/L%27Age_d%27Or" title="L&#39;Age d&#39;Or">L'Age d'Or</a></i> (<i>The Golden Age</i>) film in collaboration with Luis Buñuel</li>
<li>1931 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Persistence_of_Memory" title="The Persistence of Memory">The Persistence of Memory</a></i> (his most famous work, featuring the "melting clocks"), <i>The Old Age of William Tell</i>, and <i>William Tell and <a href="/wiki/Gradiva" title="Gradiva">Gradiva</a></i></li>
<li>1932 <i>The Spectre of Sex Appeal</i>, <i>The Birth of Liquid Desires</i>, <i>Anthropomorphic Bread</i>, and <i>Fried Eggs on the Plate without the Plate</i>. <i>The Invisible Man</i> (begun 1929) completed (although not to Dalí's own satisfaction)</li>
<li>1933 <i>Retrospective Bust of a Woman</i> (mixed media sculpture <a href="/wiki/Collage" title="Collage">collage</a>) and <i>Portrait of Gala With Two Lamb Chops Balanced on Her Shoulder</i>, <i>Gala in the Window</i></li>
<li>1934 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ghost_of_Vermeer_of_Delft_Which_Can_Be_Used_As_a_Table" title="The Ghost of Vermeer of Delft Which Can Be Used As a Table">The Ghost of Vermeer of Delft Which Can Be Used As a Table</a></i> and <i>A Sense of Speed</i></li>
<li>1935 <i>Archaeological Reminiscence of Millet's Angelus</i> and <i>The Face of <a href="/wiki/Mae_West" title="Mae West">Mae West</a></i></li>
<li>1936 <i>Autumn Cannibalism</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Lobster_Telephone" title="Lobster Telephone">Lobster Telephone</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Soft_Construction_with_Boiled_Beans_(Premonition_of_Civil_War)" title="Soft Construction with Boiled Beans (Premonition of Civil War)">Soft Construction with Boiled Beans (Premonition of Civil War)</a></i> and two works titled <i><a href="/wiki/Morphological_Echo" title="Morphological Echo">Morphological Echo</a></i> (the first of which began in 1934)</li>
<li>1937 <i><a href="/wiki/Metamorphosis_of_Narcissus" title="Metamorphosis of Narcissus">Metamorphosis of Narcissus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Swans_Reflecting_Elephants" title="Swans Reflecting Elephants">Swans Reflecting Elephants</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Burning_Giraffe" title="The Burning Giraffe">The Burning Giraffe</a></i>, <i>Sleep</i>, <i>The Enigma of Hitler</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mae_West_Lips_Sofa" title="Mae West Lips Sofa">Mae West Lips Sofa</a></i> and <i>Cannibalism in Autumn</i></li>
<li>1938 <i>The Sublime Moment</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Apparition_of_Face_and_Fruit_Dish_on_a_Beach" title="Apparition of Face and Fruit Dish on a Beach">Apparition of Face and Fruit Dish on a Beach</a></i></li>
<li>1939 <i><a href="/wiki/Shirley_Temple,_The_Youngest,_Most_Sacred_Monster_of_the_Cinema_in_Her_Time" title="Shirley Temple, The Youngest, Most Sacred Monster of the Cinema in Her Time">Shirley Temple, The Youngest, Most Sacred Monster of the Cinema in Her Time</a></i></li>
<li>1940 <i><a href="/wiki/Slave_Market_with_the_Disappearing_Bust_of_Voltaire" title="Slave Market with the Disappearing Bust of Voltaire">Slave Market with the Disappearing Bust of Voltaire</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Face_of_War" title="The Face of War">The Face of War</a></i></li>
<li>1941 <i>Honey is Sweeter than Blood</i></li>
<li>1943 <i>The Poetry of America</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Geopoliticus_Child_Watching_the_Birth_of_the_New_Man" title="Geopoliticus Child Watching the Birth of the New Man">Geopoliticus Child Watching the Birth of the New Man</a></i></li>
<li>1944 <i>Galarina</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dream_Caused_by_the_Flight_of_a_Bee_Around_a_Pomegranate_a_Second_Before_Awakening" title="Dream Caused by the Flight of a Bee Around a Pomegranate a Second Before Awakening">Dream Caused by the Flight of a Bee Around a Pomegranate a Second Before Awakening</a></i></li>
<li>1944–48 <i>Hidden Faces</i>, a novel</li>
<li>1945, <i><a href="/wiki/Basket_of_Bread" title="Basket of Bread">Basket of Bread&#160;– Rather Death than Shame</a></i> and <i>Fountain of Milk Flowing Uselessly on Three Shoes</i>; also this year, Dalí collaborated with <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Hitchcock" title="Alfred Hitchcock">Alfred Hitchcock</a> on a dream sequence to the film <i><a href="/wiki/Spellbound_(1945_film)" title="Spellbound (1945 film)">Spellbound</a></i>, to mutual dissatisfaction</li>
<li>1946 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Temptation_of_St._Anthony_(Dal%C3%AD)" title="The Temptation of St. Anthony (Dalí)">The Temptation of St. Anthony</a></i></li>
<li>1948 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Elephants" title="The Elephants">The Elephants</a></i></li>
<li>1949 <i><a href="/wiki/Leda_Atomica" title="Leda Atomica">Leda Atomica</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Madonna_of_Port_Lligat" title="The Madonna of Port Lligat">The Madonna of Port Lligat</a></i>. Dalí returned to Catalonia this year</li>
<li>1951 <i><a href="/wiki/Christ_of_Saint_John_of_the_Cross" title="Christ of Saint John of the Cross">Christ of Saint John of the Cross</a></i> and <i>Exploding Raphaelesque Head</i></li>
<li>1951 <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Cornell" title="Katharine Cornell">Katharine Cornell</a>, a portrait of the famed actress</li>
<li>1952 <i><a href="/wiki/Galatea_of_the_Spheres" title="Galatea of the Spheres">Galatea of the Spheres</a></i></li>
<li>1954 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Disintegration_of_the_Persistence_of_Memory" title="The Disintegration of the Persistence of Memory">The Disintegration of the Persistence of Memory</a></i> (begun in 1952), <i><a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_(Corpus_Hypercubus)" title="Crucifixion (Corpus Hypercubus)">Crucifixion (Corpus Hypercubus)</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Young_Virgin_Auto-Sodomized_by_the_Horns_of_Her_Own_Chastity" title="Young Virgin Auto-Sodomized by the Horns of Her Own Chastity">Young Virgin Auto-Sodomized by the Horns of Her Own Chastity</a></i></li>
<li>1955 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sacrament_of_the_Last_Supper" title="The Sacrament of the Last Supper">The Sacrament of the Last Supper</a></i>, <i>Lonesome Echo</i>, record album cover for comedian <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Gleason" title="Jackie Gleason">Jackie Gleason</a></li>
<li>1956 <i><a href="/wiki/Still_Life_Moving_Fast" class="mw-redirect" title="Still Life Moving Fast">Still Life Moving Fast</a></i>, <i>Rinoceronte vestido con puntillas</i></li>
<li>1957 <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Santiago_el_Grande&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Santiago el Grande (page does not exist)">Santiago el Grande</a></i> oil on canvas on permanent display at Beaverbrook Art Gallery in Fredericton, NB, Canada</li>
<li>1958 <i>The Meditative Rose</i></li>
<li>1959 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Discovery_of_America_by_Christopher_Columbus" title="The Discovery of America by Christopher Columbus">The Discovery of America by Christopher Columbus</a></i></li>
<li>1960 <i>Composición Numérica</i> (de fond préparatoire inachevé), in acrylic and oil on canvas</li>
<li>1960 Dalí began work on the <a href="/wiki/Dal%C3%AD_Theatre_and_Museum" title="Dalí Theatre and Museum">Teatro-Museo Gala Salvador Dalí</a> and <i>Portrait of <a href="/wiki/Juan_de_Pareja" title="Juan de Pareja">Juan de Pareja</a>, the Assistant to Velázquez</i></li>
<li>1961 <i>El Triomf I el Rodoli de la Gala I en Dali</i>, a composite of Dalí's favorite graphical motifs which was gifted to his friend Fages de Climent, and later sold, leading to a cosmetic and beauty company gaining access to it and the rights to build Elevatione by Salvador Dali, a luxury cosmetic and makeup company in 2013.</li>
<li>1963–1964 <i>They Will All Come from Saba</i> a work in water color depicting the <a href="/wiki/Magi" title="Magi">Magi</a>, at St. Petersburg's Dalí Museum</li>
<li>1965 Dalí donated a <a href="/wiki/Gouache" title="Gouache">gouache</a>, ink and pencil drawing of the Crucifixion to the <a href="/wiki/Rikers_Island" title="Rikers Island">Rikers Island</a> jail in New York City. The drawing hung in the inmate dining room from 1965 to 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-jail_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jail-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1965 <i>Dalí in New York</i></li>
<li>1967 <i><a href="/wiki/Tuna_Fishing" class="mw-redirect" title="Tuna Fishing">Tuna Fishing</a></i></li>
<li>1969 <a href="/wiki/Chupa_Chups" title="Chupa Chups">Chupa Chups</a> logo</li>
<li>1969 <i>Improvisation on a Sunday Afternoon</i>, television collaboration with the British <a href="/wiki/Progressive_rock" title="Progressive rock">progressive rock</a> group <a href="/wiki/Nirvana_(UK_band)" class="mw-redirect" title="Nirvana (UK band)">Nirvana</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>
<li>1970 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hallucinogenic_Toreador" title="The Hallucinogenic Toreador">The Hallucinogenic Toreador</a></i>, purchased in 1969 by <a href="/wiki/Reynolds_and_Eleanor_Morse" title="Reynolds and Eleanor Morse">Reynolds and Eleanor Morse</a> before it was completed<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>
<li>1972 <i>Gala</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Helena_Devlin_Diakonoff_-_dit.,_GALA" class="mw-redirect" title="Helena Devlin Diakonoff - dit., GALA">Elena Ivanovna Diakonova – dit., GALA</a> Bronze sculpture, piece unique</i><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1973 <i>Les Diners de Gala</i>, an ornately illustrated cookbook</li>
<li>1976 <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gala_Contemplating_the_Mediterranean_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Gala Contemplating the Mediterranean Sea (page does not exist)">Gala Contemplating the Mediterranean Sea</a></i></li>
<li>1977 <i>Dalí's Hand Drawing Back the Golden Fleece in the Form of a Cloud to Show Gala Completely Nude, Very Far Away Behind the Sun</i> (<a href="/wiki/Stereoscopy" title="Stereoscopy">stereoscopical</a> pair of paintings)</li>
<li>1981 <i>Femme à la tête de rose</i> In 1935 Dali painted the Woman with the head of roses homage to the verse of René Crevel appeared in the <a href="/wiki/Minotaure" title="Minotaure">surrealist magazine "Le Minotaure"</a>: "But it appears and it is spring. A ball of flowers will serve as his head. His  is both the hive and the bouquet..." Decades later, he made it an elevated sculpture and Supported by crutches.This beautiful phytomorphic creature expresses both grace and rigidity, femininity and animality.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1983 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Swallow%27s_Tail" title="The Swallow&#39;s Tail">The Swallow's Tail</a></i>, Dalí's final painting</li>
<li>2003 <i lang="es" title="Spanish language text"><a href="/wiki/Destino" title="Destino">Destino</a></i>, an animated short film originally a collaboration between Dalí and <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a>, is released. Production on <i lang="es" title="Spanish language text">Destino</i> began in 1945.</li></ul>
<h2><span id="Dal.C3.AD_museums_and_permanent_exhibitions"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Dalí_museums_and_permanent_exhibitions">Dalí museums and permanent exhibitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Dalí museums and permanent exhibitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current">Current</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Current">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Art Bank, private exhibition – <a href="/wiki/Pargas" title="Pargas">Pargas</a>, Finland</li>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dal%C3%AD_%E2%80%93_Die_Ausstellung_am_Potsdamer_Platz&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dalí – Die Ausstellung am Potsdamer Platz (page does not exist)">Dalí – Die Ausstellung am Potsdamer Platz</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dal%C3%AD_%E2%80%93_Die_Ausstellung_am_Potsdamer_Platz" class="extiw" title="de:Dalí – Die Ausstellung am Potsdamer Platz">de</a>&#93;</span> – <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>, Germany, a museum with a permanent exhibition</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dal%C3%AD_Theatre_and_Museum" title="Dalí Theatre and Museum">Dalí Theatre and Museum</a> – <a href="/wiki/Figueres" title="Figueres">Figueres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a>, Spain, holds the largest collection of Dalí's work</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dal%C3%AD_Universe" title="Dalí Universe">Dalí Universe</a> – London, England, holds a significant collection</li>
<li>Dali17 – <a href="/wiki/Monterey,_California" title="Monterey, California">Monterey, California</a>, US, permanent exhibition</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Espace_Dal%C3%AD" class="mw-redirect" title="Espace Dalí">Espace Dalí</a> – <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, France, holds a significant collection</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Castle_of_P%C3%BAbol" title="Castle of Púbol">Gala Dalí House-Museum</a> – <a href="/wiki/Castle_of_P%C3%BAbol" title="Castle of Púbol">Castle of Púbol</a> in <a href="/wiki/P%C3%BAbol" title="Púbol">Púbol</a>, Catalonia, Spain</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Morohashi_Museum_of_Modern_Art" title="Morohashi Museum of Modern Art">Morohashi Museum of Modern Art</a> - <a href="/wiki/Kitashiobara,_Fukushima" title="Kitashiobara, Fukushima">Kitashiobara, Fukushima</a>, Japan, holds a significant collection</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Museo_Nacional_Centro_de_Arte_Reina_Sofia" class="mw-redirect" title="Museo Nacional Centro de Arte Reina Sofia">Museo Nacional Centro de Arte Reina Sofia</a> (Reina Sofia Museum) – Madrid, Spain, holds a significant collection</li>
<li>Museum-Gallery Xpo: Salvador Dali – <a href="/wiki/Bruges" title="Bruges">Bruges</a>, Belgium, permanent exhibition</li>
<li>Salvador Dalí Gallery – <a href="/wiki/San_Juan_Capistrano,_California" title="San Juan Capistrano, California">San Juan Capistrano, California</a>, US; holds a significant collection</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Salvador_Dal%C3%AD_House_Museum" title="Salvador Dalí House Museum">Salvador Dalí House Museum</a> – <a href="/wiki/Port_Lligat" class="mw-redirect" title="Port Lligat">Port Lligat</a>, Catalonia, Spain</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Salvador_Dali_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Salvador Dali Museum">Salvador Dali Museum</a> – <a href="/wiki/St_Petersburg,_Florida" class="mw-redirect" title="St Petersburg, Florida">St Petersburg, Florida</a>, contains the collection of <a href="/wiki/Reynolds_and_Eleanor_Morse" title="Reynolds and Eleanor Morse">Reynolds and Eleanor Morse</a>, and over 1500 works by Dalí, including seven large "masterworks"</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Former">Former</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Former">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Dal%C3%AD_Universe" title="Dalí Universe">Dalí Universe</a> – <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>, Italy (permanently closed)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rikers_Island" title="Rikers Island">Rikers Island</a> jail – <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, the unlikeliest venue for Dalí's work. A sketch of the <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion" title="Crucifixion">Crucifixion</a> that he donated to the jail in 1965 hung in the inmate dining room for 16 years before it was moved to the prison lobby for safekeeping. The drawing was stolen from that location in March 2003 and has not been recovered.<sup id="cite_ref-jail_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jail-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_temporary_exhibitions">Major temporary exhibitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Salvador_Dal%C3%AD&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Major temporary exhibitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><i>The Dalí Renaissance: New Perspectives on His Life and Art after 1940</i> (2005) <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Museum_of_Art" title="Philadelphia Museum of Art">Philadelphia Museum of Art</a><sup id="cite_ref-Taylor_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2018, a traveling museum exhibition focusing on Dalí's illustrations for Dante's "Divine Comedy" premiered at the Hilliard University Art Museum in Lafayette, Louisiana.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> The exhibition titled "Salvador Dalí's Stairway to Heaven" will be touring the United States through 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>

<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Ian Gibson (1997). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.nytimes.com/books/first/g/gibson-dali.html"><i>The Shameful Life of Salvador Dalí</i></a>. W. W. Norton &amp; Company.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Shameful+Life+of+Salvador+Dal%C3%AD&amp;rft.pub=W.+W.+Norton+%26+Company&amp;rft.date=1997&amp;rft.au=Ian+Gibson&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.nytimes.com%2Fbooks%2Ffirst%2Fg%2Fgibson-dali.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASalvador+Dal%C3%AD" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/> Gibson found out that "Dalí" (and its many variants) is an extremely common surname in Arab countries like <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> or <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. On the other hand, also according to Gibson, Dalí's mother's family, the Domènech of <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">Barcelona</a>, had Jewish roots.</span>
